DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
FIFTH ROAD REHABILITATION AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
I. LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrowers               Dominican Republic.
Amount:                 US$79.0 million equivalent.
Terms:                  20 years with 5 years of grace, at the standard
variable interest rate.
Project Objectives:    The key objectives of the project are:  (a) bring
the highway network to acceptable operating
standards through a program of road rehabilitation
and periodic and routine maintenance; (b) improve
the institutional capability of the Secretariat of
Public Works (SEOPC) to plan and maintain the road
system; (c) increase the availability of road
operating equipment; (d) improve the quality of
pavements; and (e) strengthen the domestic
construction industry.
Project Description:   The project will finance the first three years of a
five-year highway investment plan agreed with the
Bank. The project would comprise: (a)
rehabilitation of about 830 km of roads and
supervision of these works; (b) a program of
periodic maintenance to be executed by private
contractors and SEOPC; (c) a program of workshop
reconstruction; (d) equipment rehabilitation
including purchase of spare parts; (e) road
signalling and marking and vehicle weight control;
and (f) technical assistance for training equipment
operators, pavement technology transfer, road
maintenance management, a rural roads inventory, an
urban transport study, a construction industry
study, general training and scholarships; and
engineering services for road design.
Project Benefits:       The project will result in a substantial improvement
of the physical condition of the highway network and
a significant reduction in vehicle operating costs.
improved access to markets and further tourism
development. All subprojects included in the
project and the five-year plan were subject to an
economic analysis which discarded any investment
with a rate of return below 12%. Other benefits
include improving the institutional setup,
increasing availability of equipment and
productivity of workshops and introducing an
improved system of road maintenance, thereby
eliminating the need for repeated road
rehabilitation cycles.



- 2 -
Proiect Risks:          The main project risks ares  (a) slow implementation
of the road maintenance program. To minimize this
risk, the proposed project includes an agreed five-
year maintenance program supported by extensive
training in pavement technology, and consultants for
the maintenance management system. Periodic
maintenance will be largely contracted out and the
maintenance organization strengthened both at the
central level and in the field; (b) possible delays
in starting civil works because of insufficient
engineering design capability. First year designs
were completed before negotiations and the project
includes funds for preparation of engineering
designs by outside firms; (c) delays in issuing bid
documents and selection of inexperienced
contractors. Standard bidding documents were
completed and stricter criteria for prequalification
of contractors were agreed during negotiations; (d)
high staff turnover. While the project provides
some incentives in the form of training programs,
the reduction of staff turnover has to be resolved
in the context of overall Government policy in the
public sector.

II. THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
A. The Setting
2.01     The Dominican Republic covers the eastern two thirds of the island
of Hispaniola in the Greater Antilles (Map IBRD 22366). It has an area of
about 48,700 km2 and a population of close to 7 million, which is growing
at an annual rate of 2.4Z. The country is crossed by four mountain ranges
separated by fertile valleys. The major population centers and
agricultural production are in and around the Cibao Valley. The economy
relies heavily on the transport system to provide an adequate and efficient
access to markets for primary agricultural production and mining output.
Domestic cargo traffic consists mainly of exports commodities to the ports
(sugar, coffee, cacao and minerals) and internal trade in agricultural
products from the Cibao Valley and industrial and consumer goods from Santo
Domingo. Tourism, which is now second only to agriculture in contribution
to exports, depends heavily on transport infrastructure for future growth.
2.02     The Dominican Republic has developed a relatively dense transport
network which includes about 19,000 km of roads (of which about 14,000 km
are rural roads), 1,700 km of railway, 14 ports (two major ones) and five
principal airports (two with scheduled international service). The
transport sector accounts for about 6Z of GDF. Highway transport is the
most widely used mode, and the network provides access to all major
producing areas. Because of the relatively small size of the country, the
location of its resources and its economic structure, average haul
distances are short (about 100 km). Sugar cane is the only commodity
transported mainly by rail, which is operated by the State Sugar Council.
B. Institutional Framework
2.03     The Secretariat for Public Works and Communications (SEOPC, Chart
I) is responsible for maintaining the road network, supervising
construction and reconstruction of roads, and transport policy and planning
(with the exception of regulation of passenger transport, which is in the
hands of the Secretariat of the Presidency - Presidencia - and freight
cargo rates, which are determined by the Secretariat of Industry and
Commerce). The transport functions of SEOPC are divided among two
Subsecretariatst the Subsecretariat for Roads is responsible for road
design, maintenance of the network and supervision of construction, and the
Subsecretariat for Land Transport is responsible for traffic regulation,
vehicle inspection, eriver licensing, and vehicle weight control. The
organizational setup of the SEOPC is reasonable. However, the General
Directorate of Road Maintenance (DGM) within the Subsecretariat of Roads is
weak: first, its effective number of employees differs from its payroll
list; second, its personnel is not adequately trained; third. it has no
valid plan for staff deployment among zones and tasks; and, fourth, it has
not pursued consistent road maintenance plans. As part of the proposed
project, the DGM will be strengthened and reorganized, with staff
redeployed as required, a training program initiated and a specific medium-
term road maintenance plan developed and executed (See paras. 2.08-2.11 and
3.06-3.08).



-4-
2.04     The General Directorate of Planning and Programming (DGPP) within
the Subsecretariat of Roads is responsible for investment planning, project
budgeting, and maintaining the transport sector data base including road
physical data, vehicle operating costs, and tr'ffic and vehicle fleet
characteristics. A major effort has been made in recent years through
technical assistance and training to upgrade staff knowledge and install
improved planning systems. Indeed, the investment planning area is now
functioning smoothly and the quality of staff has improved considerably.
The proposed 1992-96 road investment plan has been prepared by DGPP staff
with assistance by consultants financed under the Third Road Reconstruction
Project (Loan 2609-DO). DGPP staff is now able to update the investment
program and conduct new feasibility studies as necessary. In order to
strengthen DGPP's ability to carry out its functions, it should also absorb
the traffic count section, now within the Subsecretariat of Land Transport
(para. 2.15).
2.05     The budget of the SEOPC is approved annually by Congress based on
submission by the Presidencia. Budget allocations to the road sector have
been reasonably stable as a percentage of total Government expenditure:
they have varied between 92 and 11? in the period 1980-1988 (Table 1).
This level is reasonable. However, the process of preparing the road
construction budget has been increasingly shifting away from the SEOPC to
Presidencia, giving the latter substantial control over the selection of
roads to be rehabilitated. Secondly, the budget contains less funds than
would normally be required for road maintenance and more than normal for
reconstrujction (necessitated by inadequate past maintenance practices--see
paras. 2.09-2.11). Through an agreement on a five-year highway
rehabilitation and reconstruction plan, the proposed project is designed to
shift the budgeting process for road rehabilitation back to the SEOPC and
give it a determining role in road selection. The project also emphasizes
an increased level of expenditure for road maintenance, both routine and
periodic (paras. 3.07-3.08).
2.06     The SEOPC also plays a role in the airport and port subsectors.
The Secretary of Public Works is ex officio chairman of the Airport
Commission, which overseas airport operations and investments and approves
the budget for these purposes. The Secretary of Public Works is also the
President of the Port Commission, which is the governing body of the
Dominican Port Authority (APD). In addition, SEOPC supervises the design
and constrtction of the ports through its Ports and Wharfs Department and
conducts port dredging operations.
C. The Transport Subsectors
(a) Roads
(i)    General
2.07     The road network consists of about 4,900 km of highways and about
14,000 km of rural roads. Of the total highway network about 3,800 km are
paved or being paved and about 1,100 km are unpaved (Tables 1 and 2). The
highway network comprises 1,073 km (22?) of primary roads, 2,302 km (46Z)
of regional and 1,569 km (32Z) of local roads. All primary roads, and
about 70? of regional and local ro,ds, are paved (Table 3). Of the total



-5-
rural road network, 700 km are either paved or being paved. Recent surveys
conducted by the SEOPC show that about 1,300 km (27S) of the highway
network are in bad condition and need rehabilitation or reconstruction.
This is an improvement from the more than 2,000 km reported in need of
major repairs in 1985. The improved state is due to the major effort
mounted by the Government in recent years to rehabilitate existing
highways. In the period 1987-89 the reconstruction of 517 km of highways
was completed and 448 km are still under construction. The effort was
supported by funds provided under the Second Road Rehabilitation Project
(Loan 1784-DO), now completed, and the Third Road Reconstruction Project,
Loan 2609-DO, which is nearing completion. There is no up-to-date
inventory of the rural road network.
(ii)   Road Maintenance
2.08     Maintenance of the highways and rural road networks (Annexes 1-2)
is carried out by the General Directorate of Maintenance (DGM) with two
sub-directorates at the central level: one for highway and rural road
maintenance and another for operations. with a common organization at the
district ievel. DGM also supervises road maintenance equipment and
operates all workshops (Annex 3). DGM employs about 6,000 people, of which
1,200 are at the central level, with the rest working in four maintenance
districts (Santo Domingo, Santiago, La Vega and Azua). The districts are
further divided into 21 sub-districts, each employing some 200 people. The
number of workers at the DGM appears excessive, but a closer examination
reveals that only about 3.500 people are actually working in the districts
in maintenance activities (Annex 4).   This number ought to be sufficient
to carry out the required maintenance work. Agreement was reached during
negotiations that by December 31, 1991, SEOPC will furnish to the Bank a
plan on organization and administration of personnel for DGM, detailing the
positions assigned to road maintenance activities and their costs.
2.09     Maintenance planning is not generally practiced.  DGM has a
planning unit which is using a highway maintenance model developed by
consultants under the previous Bank loans. However, this planning unit is
understaffed and does not cover the rural road system. Furthermore, there
is no quantitative input from the field operating units to the planning
unit. Another difficulty originates from the fact that all funds for
purchase of materials are controlled centrally within SEOPC and are not
assigned directly to DGM. Finally, release of approved funds by the
Ministry of Finance is often delayed. As a result, the planning function
is limited in reality to budgeting for ad-hoc works without following
established budget procedures and without keeping adequate records.
Technical assistance will be provided under the project to implement the
existing road maintenance management system, which would also improve the
budgetary control and monitoring (paras. 3.18-3.19 and Annexes 6-7).
Agreement was reached during negotiations that the funds allocated to road
maintenance will be provided promptly to DGM. Agreement was also reached
that, as part of the implementation program, SEOPC will strengthen the
planning unit of DGM by adding an engineer and an assistant engineer, and
nominate an engineer in charge of highway maintenance irn each of the 21
sub-districts. These actions should take place no later than December 31,
1991.



- 6 -
2.10     The rural road network of about 14,000 km is also maintained by
the SEOPC. However, the planning organization for rural roads at the
central level is practically non-existent and works are essentially limited
to projects financed by IDB and USAID. From 1980 to 1984, IDB financed the
construction of 700 km of feeder roads and is currently financing the
reconstruction of an additional 1,000 km with two loans. A new project in
preparation will finance reconstruction of another 1,000 km. Between 1982
and 1987 USAID financed the rehabilitation of 900 km of feeder roads and is
now financing routine maintenance of 2,000 km. Such maintenance is carried
out by local part-time workers, nominated by the community, who are paid a
modest amount by the SEOPC and use tools and equipment provided by the
USAID program. This scheme has been successful. In addition, SEOPC has
about eleven road teams assigned to maintenance of rural roads, capable of
maintaining an additional 3,500 km per year. Given the results of a CIDA
study that estimated the viable rural road network at about 9,000 km, it
appears that over one third of the viable rural roads do not receive any
maintenance. The implementation program agreed during negotiations
includes a provision to create a separate planning and control tunit for
rural roads within DGM by December 31, 1991.
2.11     Road maintenance practices in the Dominican Republic continue to
be deficient. The faults lies principally in inadequate planning and
deficient organization of work and in insufficient budgetary allocation.
Maintenance expenditures for 1989 were about RD$48.5 million, compared to a
desirable level of RD$75.0 million. The budget shortfall is mainly in
materials, where only about a quarter of the required amount is provided by
the budget. On the other hand, the budget allocation for personnel exceeds
the amount required because provision is made for workers who do not
perform actual work. The shortfall in maintenance affects mainly surface
treatment, thin overlays and resealing where materials play an important
role. As a result of deficient maintenance, many roads deteriorate and
eventually require rehabilitation and reconstruction. The improvement of
maintenance practices is the focus of the proposed project, which includes
an agreement on adequate funding levels, administrative arrangements to
ensure that allocated funds are indeed used for maintenance work, and
technical assistance to reorganize and train the planning and
Implementation units of the DGM (see paras. 3.07-3.08 and 3.17).
(iii) Highway Design and Construction
2.12     Highway engineering design is the responsibility of the General
Directorate of Roads (DGC) within the SEOPC. Most highway design is done
in-house. The quality of SEOPC's engineers is reasonable, although better
pay in the private sector results in high staff turnover. Supervision of
civil works is the responsibility of the General Directorate for
Supervision (DGSFO), which is assisted by outside consultants assigned to
individual projects. Construction is normally carried out by contractors,
both local and foreign, although SEOPC has some construction capacity which
it reserves mainly for emergency works.
2.13     At present, the private road construction industry has five big
firms well equipped for road construction and rehabilitation works; some
20-25 medium-size firms with equipment to carry out rehabilitation works on
short sections of highways and feeder roads; and more than 40 small
companies of individual contractors with limited owned or rented equipment.



Services cf the small companies are retained for minor road maintenance or
rehabilitation works. Road equipment owned by most companies is mostly of
older vintage because contractors prefer, due to high import taxes, to buy
used reconditioned equipment from overseas suppliers. Rented equipment is
in short supply and prevailing rental rates are high.
2.14     In the past, most government contracts were awarded directly
without competitive bidding. Since 1978, bidding has become common,
together with contract negotiation. Bank's insistence on institutionalized
competitive bidding further accelerated the process of enhancing
competition. Also, in the past some contracts were awarded to private
contractors who did not have adequate experience and the capacity to carry
out the works at the required pace and in adherence to acceptable quality
standards. To ensure acceptable quality of civil works, the format
relating to prequalification of contractors has been revised (paras. 2.33
and 4.11). Furthermore, the proposed project includes a study of the
construction industry to identify the main constraints to development of
the industry, review procurement procedures and contracting practices,
choice of technology, training, equipment financing and legal environment.
Terms of reference for the study are included in Annex 8.
(iv)   Traffic, Vehicle Fleet and Axle Load Regulation
2.15     The last extensive road traffic count in the Dominican Republic
was carried out in 1985. The unit within the Directorate of Land Transport
(DGTT) which is responsible for traffic counts has lost most of its staff,
its equipment has broken down and budget allocations have been insufficient
for it to carry out its tasks. As part of the preparation of the proposed
project, traffic counts were carried out on a case-by-case basis by the
Pavement Division of the DGPP, which also maintains the transport sector
data base. Agreement was reached during negotiations that, as part of the
implementation program, the unit responsible for traffic counts will be
transferred to the DGPP by December 31, 1991, and that '.t would be properly
staffed and budgeted for.
2.16     The vehicle 'leet has been expanding at 6% p.a. since 1980 and
totals about 200,000 (Table 4). The fleet consists of 59% cars, 33Z
trucks, 6% buses and 21 other vehicle. Of the various categories, buses
have increased fastest reflecting the urbanization trends and the small and
inadequate fleet in the base year. Consumption of both gasoline and diesel
shows a similar increase to that of.the vehicle fleet, at about 7% p.a.
(Table 5). The increases in the fleet came despite high level of customs
duties, which range from 1402 for small cars to 200 for large cars.
Import taxes on trucks are at 45% and for buses at 82%.
2.17     Axle weight control was first addressed under a law in 1967.  The
law established maximum dimensions of vehicles; authorized the Government
to establish regulations for dimensions and weight of the different types
of road vehicles; authorized the DGTT to implement these regulations; and
established sanctions to be applied to violators. The law, however, did
not establish norms for maximum weight or axle loads. In JuJy 1970, the
Government issued a decree which established maximum single and double axle
loads at 9,000 kg and 14,500 kg respectively and established fines for
overweight vehicles. Further modification was introduced in February 1976
by the DGTT allowing a maximum load of 13,000 kg for single axles (four



- 8 -
wheels) and 21,000 kg for tandem axles of eight wheels with a provision for
a reduction of these maxima if the axles have fewer wheels.  These higher
limits are consistent with recent Bank-sponsored research in developing
countries.  Higher axle loads allow truck operators to lower their costs
per ton carried, whiie requiring the road authority to build thicker
pavements.  The optimum for the economy as a whole is likely to be reached
if the legal limit is set in the range 12-13 tons for single axles.
2.18     To control vehicle axle loads. SEOPC had constructed five
permanent weighing stations. Only one of these stations is operational at
present: weighing equipment has never been installed at two stations and
it is damaged and inoperative at two others (each station requires 9
operators and a supervisor). Experience with vehicle weight control in
other countries demonstrates, however, that the policy is better enforced
when portable scales are used. The latter prevents vehicle operators from
using their knowledge of the location of the weighing stations to evade
control. Agreement was reached during negotiations that, as part of the
implementation program, SEOPC will purchase the portable scales and put
them in operation no later than the end of 1991.
(v)    Passenger Transport
2.19     Urban passenger transport in the Dominican Republic is provided
essentially by private enterprises and controlled by the Office of Land
Transport (OTT) within the Presidencia. The OTT exercises substantial
control over urban transport in Santo Domingo: it issues permits, inspects
vehicles and assigns routes and frequencies. The tariff level in recent
years was very low: in mid-1989 it was set at the equivalent of US$0.08
for individual operators and cooperatives and US$0.04 for the Government-
owned entity, ONATRATE. The tariff level was too low to cover operating
costs and capital replacement. As a result, most of the vehicles used in
passenger transport are old and in bad condition. Recently, the Government
took steps to alleviate some of these pressures and authorized a
substantial increases in rates to RDS1.50-2.00 (US$0.12-0.15). ONATRATE's
revenues in 1990 overed only about 30? of its operating costs (excluding
depreciation) and the company relied on the Government to cover its losses
and replace its aging fleet of about 180 buses. The Government also
provided incentives to the urban transport cooperatives by providing loans
on preferential terms (no down payment and no interest). Despite such
recent financing for 300 buses, urban passenger transport remains grossly
inadequate, especially in the capital city of Santo Domingo. There are no
fixed passenger stations, and frequencies and assigned routes are not
observed. The equipment is, by and large, obsolete and insufficient.
4ajor improvements in street infrastructure, including drainage and
signalling, are also required. The magnitude of the issue is such that it
goes beyond the scope of the proposed project. However, funds have been
included in the project for a major study of urban transport demand, street
infrastructure, and improvements required in transport operations and
policies (see terms of reference in Annex 9).
2.20     Inter-urban passenger traffic is provided by eight major
companies. The OTT has to approve routes and frequencies but this
procedure is merely a formality as practically all requests are approved.
OTT does not interfere with tariff setting by the private operators and
competition works well. All companies have their own terminals and
schedules are observed.



(vi)   Freight Transport
2.21     About half of freight in the Dominican Republic is transported by
ir,dividual operators and about half by truck owners who belong to
syndicates or associations. Entry into the sector is free of Government
intervention and the various associations have been formed on a voluntary
basis. The most important associations are: (a) the National Federation
of Dominican Transport (FENATRADO), which groups together 17 syndicates and
is mainly engaged in general freight; (b) Association of Container
Transporters (ATFI), which specializes in container traffic; and (c)
Association of Transporters of Petroleum which has a practical monopoly on
transport of petroleum products. The only strictly regulated transport is
that of petroleum products, where all vehicles need an operating permit,
are assigned to specific routes and are subject to fixed rates.
2.22     For general freight the Ministry of Industry and Commerce, in
consultation with the DGPP, sets up maximum rates for each pair of -rigin
and destination. The system has been in existence since 1974. Since 1977
the official freight rates have increased roughly in line with the CPI. In
reality, actual tariffs quoted are below the maximum levels set by the
Government. A recent study by consultants (Delcan, RJA, SAE, SERCITEC,
Canada-Dominican Republic) found that the present maximum rates provide a
substantial (20Z) margin over the observed transport cost and, therefore,
do not restric  competition or prevelit operators from entering the market.
DGPP has adopted a new methodology, recommended by the study, to compute
vehicle operating costs and set tariffs.
(vii) Fuel Prices and Road User Charges
2.23     The main sources of user charges in the road sector have been
taxes on petroleum products. Table 7 summarizes the total user charges for
the period 1985-89 and compares them to the total spending on the highway
network. Since actual maintenance cost have been below the levels required
to maintain the network in good condition, an adjustment was introduced to
bring the maintenance budget to its required level. As the table
demonstrates, user charges in 1986 covered maintenance as well as almost
all construction costs. However, because of the lag in adjustment of
gasoline prices and the lack of any change in diesel prices, fuel tares on
transport uses which exceeded RD$200 million in 1986, were a negative RD$32
million in 1989 (total fuel taxes on all uses declined from RD$476 taillion
in 1986 to a negative RD$200 million in 1989). As a result, total road
user charges for 1989 were estimated at RD$14 million, which is less than
the RD$75 million required just for maintenance. The situation was
reversed following the major adjustments in the prices'of diesel and
gasoline (see below).
2.24     Petroleum product prices are controlled by the Government.  From
1985 to 1989 these price eroded in real terms (Table 6). In mid-1990
gasoline was priced at RD$ 6.00 (US$0.79) per gallon and diesel at
RD$2.50(US$0.33) per gallon. The ex-refinery prices were RD$5.40 (US$0.71)
and RD$2.29 (US$0.30), respectively. This compared with a border price in
April 1990 of US$0.60 per gallon of gasoline and US$0.55 per gallon of
Diesel. While there were nominal (not earmarked) taxes on both products,
in reality the refinery was compensated for losses incurred when import
price and processing costs exceeded ex-refinery prices. The situation
I



- 10 -
changed drastically since mid-1990 when the Government took a decision to
allign the ex-refinery price with international price levels. In several
subsequent tariff actions the prices of diesel and gasoline were raised to
the present level of RD$13.50 (US$1.04) per gallon of diesel and RD$20.00
(US$1.54) for gasoline. This is 50Z and 1202, respectively, over border
prices and should generate about US$140 million in tax revenues from
transport users, a level sufficient to cover all road maintenance and
construction costs.
2.25     Agreement was reached during negotiations that the Government will
maintain dieFel and gasoline prices at a level sufficient to cover at least
their CIF cost-Santo Domingo. The Government will also take all actions as
may be required to ensure that the costs of road maintenance are borne by
road users.
(b) Ports
2.26     There are fourteen commercial ports in the Dominican Republic, of
which 10 serve foreign trade, one serves tourism and three serve domestic
coastal traffic. Two ports are administered privately and provide services
only to their own traffic: La Romana (Central Romana Corporation) and Cabo
Rojo (Ideal Dominicana, S.A.). The Government intends to have the
Dominican Port .- thority (APD) eventually administer all public sector
ports, of which aix are on the Caribbean Sea and six on the Atlantic Ocean.
In the meantime, the APD manages the five largest ports and the General
Directorate of Customs manages the rest. The SEOPC is supervising the
civil works done at the ports, including the US$47.8 million improvement of
the Haina port, financed with a US$35 million loan from the IDB. The
supervision function is to be transferred in the future to the APD. The
SEOPC also handles port dredging activities.
2.27     Total tonnage at the ports exceeded 10 million in 1989.  The most
important port in terms of traffic is Haina, just outside Santo Domingo,
which handled in the period 1987-89 about 702 of all traffic (Table 8).
The overall number of ports is excessive and partial closure could result
in substantial cost savings. At the same time, the Puerto Plata port could
be further developed to become a major tourism port as well as to handle
additional traffic from northern routes, for which the port offers economic
advantages.
(c) Air ,ran port
2.28     There are 44 airports in total but only five principal airports
for international traffic: Las Americas (Santo Domingo), and Puerto Plata
(scheduled flights), and La Romana, Santiago and Punta Cana (chartered
flights). The first two have runways of over 3000 m long and are designed
to accept B-747s.  La Romana airport is operated by La Romano Corporation
for international charter flight. Its runway is paved and 900 m long.
Santiago and Punta Cana airports are also paved and have runways of 1,600
and 2300 m long, respectively. Although La Romana and Punta Cana are
privately owned, the Government provides services of customs, immigration,
security, sanitation, and air traffic control. Santo Domingo international
airport is by far the most important: it handles about 75? of all
passenger traffic estimated at 2.5 million in 1988, and 922 of all freight
traffic. It is followed by Puarto Plata which bandles 23? and 71,
respectively.



- 11 -
2.29     International services are provided by numerous international and
one Dominican airline. There are no scheduled domestic flights, and all
domestic traffic is provided by air taxis carrying five passengers. With
the projected expansion in tourism, arrivals are expected to reach about
2.7 million in the year 2000 compared to 1.3 million in 1988. The major
increase is again expected in Santo Domingo and Puerto Plata. However,
Punts Car.d airport is likely to start offering scheduled services to
satisfy the demand generates by the large tourism development in the
eastern part of the island.
2.30     Administration of the major airports, except of the privately
owned La Romana and Punta Cana airports, is the responsibility of the
Airport Commission. There are 37 airstrips or aerodromes with limited
facilities, which are operated by the General Directorate of Civil
Aeronautics. The Government is funding the Airport Commission but revenues
generated by the airports (not earmarked) are more than sufficient to cover
recurrent expenditures. In 1987 revenues exceeded expenditures by RD$4.4
million and in the period 1984-87 the excess averaged RD$5.1 million. In
the same period revenues contributed about 802 of capital expenditures.
(d) Railways
2.31     The railway network is 1725 km long and almost entirely devoted to
the transport of sugar cane to the mills. Of the total railway network,
1036 km are owned and operated by the State Sugar Council. Several private
companies own and operate railways. Of these, the most important is La
Romana Corporation, which operates a network of 420 km and provides
transport to the La Romana sugar mill.
D. Experience with Past Lending in the Transport Sector
2.32     The Bank has made four loans for road reconstruction:  the First
Road Maintenance and Reconstruction Project (Loan 1316-T-DO, US$5.0
million, 1976) provided for the reconstruction of a section of the main
road connecting the capital with Santiago, the second largest city of the
Dominican Republic. Other components were a bridge rehabilitation program
and support to the Road Maintenance Directorate of SEOPC. The Project
Performance Audit Report No. 3716 (Board Doc. No. Sec. M-81-987) of 1981
indicated that project implementation generally developed as planned, but
price increases forced a reduction in the scope of the project since it
took 2.5 years to achieve congressional ratification and effectiveness of
the loan. The second Bank operation was an Emergency Road Reconstruction
Project (Loan 1783-DO, US$25.0 million. 1980). It supported reconstruction
of 150 km of hurricane-damaged roads. connecting agricultural areas in the
northwest with Santiago and in the southwest with Santo Domingo. Because
of its emergency nature, the loan was approved by the Bank before
engineering designs were available for the project. When these were later
completed and bids were received, it became evident, even after lowering
standards to obtain cost savings, that project costs would be about 502
above those estimated at loan approval. The Project Completion Report
(December 1984) indicates that beginning implementation of the project on
the basis of incomplete engineering work was the primary cause of later
delays and cost increases, but that the project, when completed, remained
economically justified. To avoid similar problems under the proposed loan,



- 12 -
detailed engineering designs for the roads to be financed during the first
year were completed before negotiations and the ac.ion plan includes target
dates for completion of final engineering for the second and third-year
project roads. The Second Road Maintenance and Reconstruction Project
(Loan 1784-DO, US$35.0 million, 1980) provided financing to reconstruct 150
km of priority primary and secondary paved roads, upgrade maintenance
operations and investigate the utility of labor-based techniques for rural
road construction. A technical assistance program for read maintenance was
also carried out under this project.
2.33     The Third Road Reconstruction Project (Loan 2609-DO, US$35.8
million, 1986) provided financing for: reconstruction of 150 km of
highways that were started under Loan 1784-DO, and about 60 km of other
high-priority highways; purchase of road maintenance equipment and spare
parts: consulting services for construction supervision; a transport
planning study; and assistance in training the Pavement Division staff of
DGPP and in organizing workshops and stores. Generally, the project has
been executed as planned, and is now nearing completion (expected closing
date is December 31, 1991). However, lack of strict criteria for
prequalification of contractors caused major delays and cost overruns in
construction of one highway where, eventually, the work had to be
transferred to,n other contractors. The existing document used by SEOPC in
the process of prequalification of contractors is very general in nature
and lacks valid quantitative criteria. The latter should be modified to
ensure that the contractors would be prequalified only after they comply
with specific criteria established to ensure that they can handle the
expected volume of work (para 4.11). A revised document, acceptable to the
Bank, was prepared by SEOPC and agreed during negotiations. The revised
document will be used to prequalify all contractors.
2.34     The transport planning study, financed under Loan 2609-DO, has
been completed and its recommendations taken into account in designing the
proposed project. Consultants employed under the loan installed a road
pavement inventory system, and an evaluating mechanism for road pavements
using the Highway Design and Maintenance Model, developed by the Bank. A
traffic projection model and a data base system for the road network and
the vehicle fleet are also in place. The technical assistance for
workshops and stores brought about substantial improvement in inventory
control. However, the workshop reorganization program continues to suffer
from lack of adequate facilities. The proposed loan includes financing for
a workshop reconstruction program.
2.35     The Bank supported the improvement of a sugar railroad network in
various areas of the country as part of the Sugar Rehabilitation Project,
Loan 1760-DO, US$25.0 million, 1979. In addition, the Bank has financed
the construction of rural access roads under various agricultural projects.
2.36     Despite recent improvements. institutional weakness. especially in
the area of road maintenance, continues to affect the road sector and
threatens the benefits obtained from past operations of both the Bank and
other financiers. High staff turnover, mainly caused by low level of wages
in the public sector, often requires repetition of training programs. From
time to time, staff departures bring about the decline of whole
organizational units. As an example, the Planning Department for Rural
Roads, supported by USAID, has practically disappeared (and the rural road



- 13 -
inventory lost), as has the traffic count section of the DGTT. The weakest
organization link is the Directorate for Road Maintenance (DGM), which is
operating without a clear organization of field units, without work norms
and specific plans, and without satisfactory budgets. The success of Bank
operations in the long run will depend on the creation of an efficient
maintenance organization. The proposed project is focused on improvements
in road maintenance practices.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Justification
3.01     The Government's strategy in the transport sector, supported by
the Bank, has focused on reconstruction of key links of the highway
network. This rehabilitation effort has accelerated in recent years, with
the Bank supporting rehabilitation of about 200 km through Loan 2609-DO,
and with the Government, using its own funds, completing reconstruction of
517 km in the period 1987-89. As a result, the highway network's condition
has improved since the last inventory was taken in 1985. Of the total
network, about 1,300 kn are currently classified as needing rehabilitation
(compared to over 2,000 km in 1985). The Goverument is aware that
transport is a critical production and marketing facilitator for the
economy, which depends heavily on production of goods. Therefore, the
Government has requested Bank support to continue the rehabilitaEion
program. The improved roads will enable year-around traffic at
substantially reduced vehicle operating costs, thereby creating incentives
for increased production and reducing outlays of scarce foreign exchange.
A highway network in good condition, adequately maintained, would not
require substantial investment to restore heavily deteriorated road links.
Finally. many road links included in the rehabilitation program are
essential to the development of tourism, especially in the northern and
eastern part of the country.
3.02     The benefits from the rehabilitation works will not be maintained
in the long term without assuring a well-executed maintenance program for
the highway network, and the setting up of an efficient organization to
plan and carry out this maintenance program; this should be the main
contribution of Bank's participation in the proposed project. The deep
changes in reorganization of the work force, the systematization of a
medium-term maintenance program, increased budgetary allocation, ano
introduction of periodic maintenance by contract, would be implemented only
with Bank involvement and close supervision. Bank participation will also
enable the annual updating of a five-vear investment plan based on changing
road condition and demand requirements. The proposed project would build
on the extensive technical assistance program started uiider the previous
Bank projects.
B. Objectives
3.03     The objectives of the proposed project are to:
(a) reduce vehicle operating costs, improve access to markets and
encourage tourism development, by rehabilitating about 830 km
of deteriorated highway links over a three-year period;



- 1.4 -
(b) improve the institutional capacity of SEOPC to maintain the
road system by introducing detailed work programs,
reorganizing the work force, providing additional funds for
road maintenance materials, and training maintenance staff:
(c) increase the availability of equipment through a detailed
equipment rehabilitation program; and
(d) improve the quality of road pavements through an extensive
technical assistance program in this area.
C. Project Description
(a) Highway Rehabilitation
3.04     The proposed project finances the initial three-year slice (1992-
1994) of SEOPC's five-year (1992-1996) program of road rehabilitation and
priority reconstruction (Tables 9-10). The five-year program would provide
for the strengthening of 1,286 kilometers of roads.   The slice to be
financed under the proposed project comprises about 834 kilometers or 65
percent of the network identified in the five-year program. It includes
425 km of rehabilitation and new paving and 408 km of overlays. The list
of all road sections included in the three and five-year programs is given
in Table 9. Agreement was reached during negotiations that SEOPC will
carry out the 1992-96 Highway Investment Program according to the list
agreed with the Bank and included in the document "Programa de
Rehabilitaci6n de Carreteras 1992-96%. Any modification or addition will
be done only after obtaining the Bank's approval. The five-year program,
as agreed with the Bank, will be updated annually and presented to the Bank
by September 30 of each year. The staff of the DGPP has been trained and
is now capable of updating the investment program according to the agreed
methodology.
3.05     The road rehabilitation component has been evaluated in the
context of a pluriannual maintenance program based on a detailed road
condition inventory prepared in 1989-90 by SEOPC and its consultants. For
the purpose of this analysis, the network has been divided into sections,
each reflecting different pavement conditions and structural
characteristics and traffic. Estimates of the type and quantity of work
required on each section served for the preparation of preliminary cost
estimates for the works required to restore the sections to as-built
condition. About 250 km of roads which were analyzed justify only routine
maintenance and were excluded from the rehabilita '.on program. The average
base cost per km is US$67,000 for rehabilitation, and US$47.000 for
overlays. The total base cost of the rehabilitation and reconstruction
program is US$47.9 million of which the Bank will finance the estimated
foreign exchange cost of US$33.5 million (70Z of total cost).
(b) Highway Maintenance
3.06     Prior Bank projects provided technical assistance to prepare and
develop a maintenance management system, andesome training was provided
especially for equipment management and workshop organization. However,



- 15 -
while a highway inventory was carried out and a system designed to
calculate budgetary requirements, the work force was never properly
organized and a detailed work program was never implemented. The budget
for materials continues to be released by SEOPC for maintenance activities
on an ad-hoc basis and equipment continues to be scarce, given the bad
condition of the fleet and the late start in equipment rehabilitation under
the on-going Bank loan.
3.07     During project preparation a detailed budget for maintenance for
the period 1992-96 was drawn up (Tables 2-10, Annex 1), using the existing
road management system. Total requirements for labor, materials and
equipment were determined using performance standards for each activity
based on approved work methods. The programn for the period covered under
the project (1992-94) is US$19.3 million equivalent for routine maintenance
(about US$1,300 per km per year) and US$18.4 million equivalent for
periodic maintenance (about US$1,240 per km per year). The required
amounts are substantially above the amounts budgeted in recent years. For
example, the routine and periodic maintenance total about RD$82 million for
1991. The comparable figure for 1989 was RD$48.5 million. In order to
supplement SEOPC's resources, the project includes financing for the
foreign exchange component (US$9.2 million) of the US$13.7 million
allocated for periodic maintenance by contract over the 1992-94 period. An
additional US$4.7 million in periodic maintenance will be done by SEOPC
though force account, bringing the total periodic maintenance to US$18.4
million.  The periodic maintenance works to be financed by the Bank will
essentially include resealing of 805 km at a cost of US$17,000 per km.
3.08     The 1992-96 Highway Maintenance Program (Programa de Mantenimiento
de Carreteras 1992-96) as specified in Tables 5-9, Annex 1, forms an
integral part of the project and was agreed during negotiations. The
Government agreed to provide the funds necessary to implement these
programs. Agreement was also reached that by September 30 of each year,
SEOPC will present to the Bank a satisfactory updating of the road
maintenance budget for the next fiscal year, and by March 31 of each year
report to the Bank the actual budget, by category of expenditure, spent for
maintenance in the preceding fiscal year.
(c) Bridge Rehabilitation:
3.09     SEOPC's proposed bridge rehabilitation and repair program includes
eight truss bridges identified and surveyed by SEOPC engineers responsible
for the maintenance of bridges. The works required to preserve the
integrity of these structures comprise repairs of the bridges' metallic
superstructure, repairs of the expansion bearings, and some minor repairs
to the abutments. The cost of this work is estimated at US$1.4 million
(Table 11).
(d) Workshop Rehabilitation
3.10     Under Loan 2609-DO, the Bank financed a stock of spare parts and
technical assistance to reorganize existing workshops. However, several
existing workshops require reconstruction to bring the existing facilities
to an acceptable standard.  Also, there is a need for construction of
several small workshops to carry out miner repairs and preventive
maintenance. Details of the program are give in Annex 3, Table 6. The



- 16 -
project includes US$1.5 million for this program. As part of the agreed
implementation program, SEOPC will create a separate superintendency for
workshops, and staff it adequately by December 31, 1991.
(e) Equipment
3.11     The current road maintenance fleet of SEOPC (Annex 3) is large
enough to carry out the 1992-96 maintenance program. However, many pieces
of equipment need to be rehabilitated before they can be put into service.
A list of equipment for rehabilitation and repair has been prepared by DGM
with the assistance of consultants (Annex 3, Table 3). The total cost of
the program, to be financed by contract with local suppliers' workshops, is
about US$4 million. In addition, the maintenance of SEOPC's road
maintenance equipment requires acquisition of a stock of spare parts. The
proposed project includes US$7.6 million for this propose. The amount is
based on a detailed calculation for each type of equipment (Annex 3,
Table 5). Minor repair programs will be carried out by SEOPC and more
major repairs by local suppliers' workshops. Purchase of new equipment
will be limited to 20 mowers for mechanical vegetation control at a cost of
US$0.6 million. This item is of special importance because rapid
vegetation growth, c-aused by the tropical climate, interferes with drainage
flows and results in major highway damage. As part of the agreed
implementation program, SEOPC will create a separate equipment management
unit within DGM by December 31, 1991.
3.12     The current signalling system is inadequate.  Except for major
traffic arteries, there are no road markings and vertical signalling is
minimal. A program for marking and signalling for the period 1992-96 has
been prepared and amounts to US$0.9 million (Annex 5). The project
provides for the materials needed for pavement marking and signalling at a
cost of US$0.6 million. The loan also finances the equipment required to
enforce axle weight control at a cost of US$0.2 million.
3.13     The project also includes the purchase of equipment for use by the
Department of Control of Earth Excavation (DCCT) within the SEOPC, which is
responsible for controlling the environmental impact of road works and
excavation (paras. 4.19 - 4.21).
(f) Supervision of Civil Works
3.14     The project will fund supervision by independent consultants of
the civil works carried out under the project to provide quality control
and ensure compliance with bid specifications. It is estimated to cost
about 8% of the total works to be contracted. The estimated base cost is
US$4.0 million.
(g) Institutional Strengthening
3.15     This component is designed to improve subsector planning and
management and to set up an efficient organization to carry out the
maintenance programs. It aims to achieve transparency in staffing for road
maintenance and transparency and adequacy of maintenance budgets and
allocation of funds through: (i) an action program agreed with SEOPC; (ii)
training for equipment operators; and (iii) technical assistance.



.17 -
MC)    Action Proaram
3.16     The action program will includes Ci) detailing through a personnel
organization and administration plan, the staff positions assigned to road
maintenance activities within the DGM,and their total costs; (ii) adding an
engineer and an assistant engineer to DGM's planning unit: (iii) nominating
an engineer in charge of highway maintenance in each of the DGM's 21 sub-
districts; (iv) creating a separate planning and control unit for rural
roads; (v) creating a superintendency for workshops; and, (vi) creating an
equipment management unit within DOM. This would complement agreement on
transparent maintenance programs and budgets (para. 3.07).
(ii) Training for Eaui2ment Operators
3.17    The high turnover of staff, who are attracted to the private
sector by higher wages, has left SEOPC with less qualified operators,
resulting in low quality of output and reduced availability of equipment.
The project includes a training program for the operators of the various
equipment. The base cost of the program is US$0.3 million.
(iii) Technical Assistance
-    Trar. fer of Pavement Technoloay to DGM and Local Contractors
3.18     One of the major factors that have prevented adequate periodic
maintenance in the Dominican Republic has been the lack of technical
expertise to carry out surface treatments and thin asphalt concrete
overlays on roads paved with bituminous materials, especially using asphalt
emulsion as binder. Bituminous surface treatments should be a normal
periodic maintenance activity to prevent deterioration and to extend the
economic life of paved roads. The lack of expertise in surface treatment
affects not only SEOPC's maintenance effort but also the domestic
construction industry, which lacks the ability to carry out technically
sound surface treatments. Loan 2609-DO included a component to help SEOPC
improve its know-how in applying road surface treatments. However, SEOPC
requested a deferment of the program because it felt that the assistance
should be accompanied by an intensive program of maintenance work.
Accordingly, the proposed project includes a technical assistance program
for technical personnel from DGM and from the domestic construction
industry. The program is described in para. 4.06 and the terms of
reference, which were agreed with the Government during negotiations, are
given in Annex 6. The base cost of the program is estimated at US$2.3
million.
-    Road Maintenance and Equipment Management
3.19     The existing road maintenance model installed by Roy Jorgensen
Associates (USA) in 1985, needs to be reviewed and brought up to date.
Also, assistance is needed in designing proper procedures to record all
maintenance expenditures, implementing the road maintenance and equipment
management system, and training DGM staff in its application. Detailed
terms of reference are included in Annex 7 and were agreed with the
Government during negotiations. The project provides US$0.8 million for
this assistance.



- 18 -
-    Updating Rural Road Inventory
3.20     No systematic maintenance of the rural road network is possible
until there is a detailed inventory of the rural road network. This
project component involves a physical survey of the existing inventory, and
a determination of which roads should be maintained and which roads should
be abandoned or turned over to local authorities. The base cost of the
study is estimated at US$0.2 million.
-    Preparation of Engineering for Highway Design
3.21     The task of completing the final engineering design for the roads
included in the Five-Year Highway Investment Program is beyond the capacity
of the SEOPC. Funds are therefore included under the project for
contracting outside consultants to assist SEOPC in carrying out the
engineering designs for roads included in the last two years of the
proposed project. This will facilitate the project's timely execution.
The total base csst is US$0.6 million.
-    Urban Transport Study
3.22     As explained in para. 2.19, the urban transport sector requires a
major analysis of population projections and distribution in Santo Domingo;
derived transport demand projections; analysis of required transport
services; changes in national and city government policies and regulations
to improve services and induce increased private sector participation;
upgrading of street infrastructure and signalling; and enforcement of
traffic regulations. Terms of reference for the study, which were agreed
with the Government during negotiations, are given in Annex 8. The cost of
the study is estimated at US$0.5 million.
-    Construction Industry Study
3.23     An analysis is required of the existing capabilities and structure
of the construction industry in order to identify the constraints on the
present typical contractor in the areas of management, equipment and
finance. The analysis should assess the effectiveness of Government
incentives to strengthen the viability of local firms. The study should
also review the existing levels of construction technology and ways to
improve it. Other areas covered by the study include: wage rates,
employment regulation, import procedures and customs duties, taxation
regime and codes and regulations affecting the construction industry.
Detailed terms of reference, which were agreed with the Government during
negotiations, are given in Annex 9. The cost ot the study is estimated at
US$0.1 million.
D. Project Costs and Financing
3.24     The total cost of the three-year (1992-94) program, including
provision for contingenc_es over the implementation period, is estimated at
RD$1,443 million (US$111.0 million equivalent), of which US$79.0 million or
71 percent of the total project cost, would be financed under the loan.
The balance of the project's cost, RD$416 million (US$32.0 million
equivalent), would be financed by the Government. The project will finance
the foreign exchange component for civil works and equipment and all of the
components, the foreign and local base costs, the provisions for physical

is shown in Table 12. The costs reflect April 1990 prices.
3.25     Project cost estimates are based ons (i) preliminary cost
estimates obtained using up-dated costs of past similar highway
resurfacing, rehabilitation and reconstruction works; (ii) preliminary
engineering for the road maintenance program; (iii) published suppliers'
current equipment and spare parts prices adjusted for inflation; and (iv)
prevailing fees for individual technical assiatance consultants (US$10,000
per man-month for expatriate consultants and US$6,000 for local). Because
the local currency has been depreciating against foreign currencies
reflecting high local inflation rate. the costing was done in foreign
currency equivalent.
3.26     Provision for physical contingencies varies according to the type
of work. For road reconstruction and rehabilitation works involving earth
works, drainage and pavement overlays, 1O0 of the contract base price has
been adopted; for repaving and periodic maintenance, 5% of the contract
base price is considered adequate. Physical contingencies are estimated at
RD$38.0 million (US$5.0 million). Provision for price escalation is based
on Bank projections that consider price index variations for manufactured
goods which are dominant with respect to highway-related goods imported to
the Dominican Republic. The rates used are tive percent for the period
1992-l996. Price contingencies total RD$110.2 million (US$14.5 million
equivalent).
3.27     Agreement was reached during negotiations that the Government will
earmark the counterpart funds needed for the project in its investment plan
for the next three years as followss 1992 - US$10.5 million equivalent;
1993 - US$10.7 million; and 1994 - US$10.8 million.
E. Economic Evaluation
3.28     An economic analysis was performed during project preparation for
each road included in the rehabilitation component of the loan. This
component has a base cost of US$47.9 million and shows an economic return
of 492 (paras. 3.29-3.32). The periodic maintenance component (US$18.4
million) was based on established engineering standards for surface
improvements (specific quantities per km per year) and sealings (every five
years). Its benefits are obvious as it prevents substantially higher costs
associated with road deterioration and consequent need for rehabilitation.
The equipment rehabilitation program (US$4.0 million) will extend the
economic life of each unit and postpone purchases of new equipment. The
rehabilitation cost of each unit of equipment does not exceed 40z of its
replacement cost, with the average cost at 2$?, which guarantees a minimum
rate of return of 12X and yields an average return of 17%.
3.29     The economic analysis of the rehabilitation component was carried
out using the Highway Design and Maintenance 1odel. The sections to be
rehabilitated were divided into links considered homogeneous in terms of
traffic levels and physical condition. For each link, traffic was
projected based on historical trends and expected population and production
growth in the region. Using engineering data and climatic conditions, as
well as traffic characteristics and cost parameters, the model simulated
the operating conditions for each link and calculated total vehicle
operating costs. The following options were examined by the model for each
road: (a) carrying out minimum maintenance at a cost of US$250 per km;



- 21 -
(b) carrying out minimum maintenance plus all required patching. The
patching cost varies according to the thickness of the road surface:
US$8.67/m2 for 50 mm and US$10.73/m2 for 75 mm; (c) routine maintenance
plus all patching plus sealing every 5 years or when 502 of the road
surface unravels, whichever comes first, at a cost of US$1.04/m2; (d)
overlays when road roughness reaches a predetermined level or every 10
years, at a cost estimated at US$7.57/mr  for a 50 mm thickness and
US$10.48/m2 for a 75 mm thickness; and (e) rehabilitation or reconstruction
when road roughness reaches a predetermined level. The cost of
rehabilitation and reconstruction was determined based on a preliminary
engineering study for each road link. A separate analysis was conducted
for unpaved roads to check whether they merit paving with double surface
treatment.
3.30     The Highway Design Model is designed to recommend the solution
which minimizes the net present value of the costs associated with each
solution including investment and maintenance costs and vehicle operating
costs. The higher the traffic level, the more rapid is the deterioratWon
in road condition, and the more likely is a higher-cost investment
alternative. The analynles resulted in the following recommended solutions:
for the 1,300 km analyzed, 250 km merit only minimum maintenance because of
low actual and projected traffic. Of the 834 km included in the project,
408 km justify overlays, 400 km justify rehabilitation, and 26 km of
unpaved roads merit paving.
3.31     The estimated cost and economic rate of return for each road link
are included in Table 9. The rates of return are based on comparing the
cost and benefits of the proposed solution with the second-best
alternative. A minimum rate of return of 12% was required for any road
rehabilitation project to be included in the program. As the tables
demonstrate, the rates of return vary from 14? to over 100X. The weighted
economic return is 49? for the roads included in the project and 362 for
the five-year rehabilitation program as a whole. Agreement was reached
during negotiations that, before undertAking any investment, the Bank's
approval will be sought if final engineering for any road link results in
cost estimates exceeding the original estimate by more than 25? in dollar
terms.
3.32     The above analysis underestimates the true economic benefits of
the road rehabilitation program because it does not quantify the additional
benefits to be derived from increased tourism activity or production due to
lower transport cost compared to the second-best alternative. It also does
not take into account the time savings accruing to passengers of private
vehicles with the increase in vehicle speed.
3.33     In the section San-Cristobal-Bani (32 km), where the traffic is
5,600 vehicles per day, a climbing lane (3.2 km long) is justified and has
been included in the program. On the other hand, the analysis showed that
completing a fourth lane in the section Pedro Brand - Piedra Blanca (42.5
km) of the mainr highway from Santo Domingo to Santiago is not justified at
the present 1evel of 9,000 vehicles per day (four lanes already exist for
the first 22 km leading north from Santo Domingo where traffic is heaviest
at 20,000 vehicles per day, and a climbing lane exists for the section
under study). The economic rate of return on widening the Pedro Brand -
Piedra Blanca section to four lanes at a cost of US$565,000 per km yields
a 1.52 return, so the project has been excluded from the program.



- 22 -
IV. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Project Execution
(a) General
4.01     The project should be carried out in about t:tree years, from the
second half of 1991 through end 1994 (Chart II). SEOPC will act as the
executing agency. General administrative coordination and relations with
the Bank will be provided by the project administrative unit within the
Department for Administration of External Resources (DARE), which was
established under the previous Bank loan and reports directly to the
Secretary of Public Works. SEOPC has appointed a qualified candidate,
acceptable to the Bank, to serve as project coordinator. Responsibility
for the preparation and execution of specific project components will be
delegated by the Secretary of Public Works to the General Directorates of
Highways (DGC), Maintenance (DGM), Supervision of Works (DGSFO). Land
Transport (DGTT), and Planning and Programming (DGPP), as has been the
practice in previous Bank-financed projects in the Dominican Republic.
(b) Civil Works
(i)    Highway ReLabilitation
4.02     Engineering designs for the civil works component will be the
responsibility of the General Directorate of Highways (DGC) within SEOPC,
while supervision of implementation will be in the hands of the General
Directorate for Supervision of Works (DGSFO). The SEOPC now has acceptable
experience in procurement of civil works, but is short of experienced
engineers and qualified field technicians. To ensure proper management and
timely monitoring and supervision of the project's civil works and road
maintenance component, SEOPC will retain consultants with funding provided
under the loan. Preparation of final engineering and bidding documents and
final cost estimates for the repaving and rehabilitation works of some 300
kilometers of roads scheduled to be contracted in the first year (1992),
was completed by SEOPC, with consultants' assistance. As part of the
implementation program agreed during negotiations, engineering design for
the second year of the project should be ready by end-1991 and for the
third year by end-1992. Funding for consultants to assist in this work is
included under the loan.
4.03     To expedite project execution, civil works contracts should be
packaged to comprise up to 100 kilometers of road works that would be
grouped, wherever possible, according to their geographical lvcation to
facilitate contractors' logistic support activities. A list of tentative
packages is provided in Table 10.
4.04     To ensure adequate quality of civil works, all roads to be
rehabilitated will be supervised by engineering consultants funded under
the project. They will ensure compliance with design specification and
report any delay in execution as well as propose on-site solutions in case
of unforeseen difficulties. They will submit quarterly progress reports.
4.05     To monitor progress of the project, SEOPC would send quarterly
reports to the Bank showing: (a) an updated listing of eligible road
projects; (b) progress on implementation of all civil works contracts and



- 23 -
other project components including latest cost estimates; (c) the status of
loan disbursements against forecasts: (d) the amount of counterpart funds
allocated to the project in the fiscal year against the armount actually
spent; and (e) the actual road maintenance budget for the' preceding year.
the budget allocated for the current Year and physical progress of the
maintenance plan for the year.
(ii)   Periodic Road Maintenance
4.06     Overall responsibility for carrying out the road maintenance
component will be vested with the General Directorate of Maintenance (DGM).
It will carry out the routine maintenance as well as 752 of the periodic
maintenance. All other periodic maintenance will be carried out by
contract. A single contractor will be selected and put in charge of all
surface improvements and sealings included in the periodic maintenance
program for the first two years (1992-93) as well as the technical
assistance component related to paving technology (para. 4.12). This
contractor will have to be able to carry out the entire process of
resurfacing, from the manufacture of asphalt products to the execution of
works.
(iii) Equipment Purchase and Rehabilitation
4.07     Acquisition of equipment and spare parts will be controlled by the
DGM, which is responsible for all the vehicle fleet. Rehabilitation of all
equipment will be done by private suppliers' workshops under DOM's
supervision. Equipment for the axle-weight control program will be
installed under the supervision of the General Directorate of Land
Transport (DGTT).
(iv) Technical Assistance Program
4.08     The responsibility for the various components will be as follows:
i)    training equipment operators - DGM
(ii)   pavement technology and surface treatments - DGM
(iii)  road maintenance management - DGM
(iv)   rural road inventory - DGM
(v)    engineering designs for proposed highways - DGC
(vi)   urban transport study - a steering committee chaired
by DGPP with representation of Presidencia and the
Municipality of Santo Domingo.
(vii)  construction industry study - DGPP
B. Procurement
4.09     Procurement will be carried out in accordance with procedures
acceptable to the Bank. Civil works contracts for road reconstruction,
rehabilitation, repaving and periodic maintenance will be procured through
international competitive bidding (ICB), except for periodic maintenance
contracts of up to US$1.5 million, which will be awarded under local
competitive bidding (LCB). A certain amount of periodic maintenance (US$
5.8 million), which will not be financed by the Bank, will be carried out
by force account. Contracts for the rehabilitation of equipment will be
awarded under ICB, except contracts not exceeding US$100,000, which will be
awarded under local competitive bidding procedures. The appraisal mission
reviewed LCB procedures and found them acceptable. Contracts for spare



- 24 _
parts for equipment repairs and rehabilitation, and materials for road
signs and markings, will be awarded through ICB, except for items costing
US$25,000 or less, and aggregating no more than US$1.3 million, which could
be purchased using local shopping. Local competitive bidding procedures
will be acceptable for the workshcps building reconstruction (US$1.5
million) and the bridge rehabilitation program (US$1.4 million), because
the works are small (no workshop will cost more than US$0.5 million) and
are unlikely to attract international bidders. All contracts under ICB, as
well as the first two contracts for civil works and for equipment under
LCB, will be subject to prior review by the Bank.
4.10     A11 civil works and equipment contracts to be procured under ICB
will follow the Bank's "Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans and
Credits, May 1985". Bidding documents for civil works will follow the
Bank's 'Sample Bidding Documents, Procurement of Works, Inter-American
Development Bank, The World Bank, September 1985". Equipment purchases
will be in accordance with the Bank's "Sample Bidding Documents,
Procurement of Goods, The Wforld Bank. Inter-American Development Bank,
March 1986". All consultancy contracts financed under the loan would be
procured following the Bank's "Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by
World Bank Borrowers and by The World Bank as Executing Agency, August
1981" and 'Sample Form of Contract for Consultants' Services, The World
Bank, March 1989".
4.11     Prequalification of civil works contractors is essential to the
bidding process and would be based on criteria which can be defined with
more precision in terms of minimum working capital, professional
experience, volume and scope of similar works. These and other criteria
would be specified by SEOPC in the prequalification documents and all
contractors (foreign and local) interested in being prequalified will be
evaluated on the basis of "meet or not meet" evaluation criteria"; that is,
only those complying with the minimum criteria would be qualified. As
stated in para. 2.33, the existing document for prequalification was
modified in consultations with the Bank and is now satisfactory. The
prequalification criteria to be used will be determined by SEOPC depending
on,
(a) The contractors' experience necessary to implement the
project as engineered and costed. Contractors would have to
submit satisfactory proof of having successfully completed,
during the last three years, road works whose annual physical
quantities and cost were similar to the annual costs required
for the project for which the contractors wish to be
prequalified. Each contractor would need to demonstrate to
the satisfaction of the Government and the Bank Lhat the
contractor has: (i) competent key personnel that would be
assigned to the project. and (ii) the necessary basic
equipment, be it owned and/or rented.
(b) Cash flow requirements. The cash flow requirements to
satisfactorily complete the works on schedule should be
determined by SEOPC based on the works schedule and the
quantity take-off of materials to be placed in the works,
estimated by the project engineer. Interested contractors
would be required to submit satisfactory proof of having



- 25 -
adequate working capital (liquid assets less liquid
liabilities) and/or available bank credit (to be certified by
a bank), to cover peak annual cashi flow needed for the works
bid on.
4.12     The major contract for providing the technical assistance for road
pavement technology and resurfacing (para. 4.06) will require a contractor
who has substantial experience in all these areas. Obviously, only major
international firms will be able to mount an adequate training team,
mobilize all the required equipment, and oossess the know-how for the
technology transfer. Such international contractors are unlikely to be
interested unless the TA contract is accompanied by a substantial civil
works contract. Also, the value of the technical assistance will be
limited if it is not done in the context of a long-term on-site training
program. Thus, the technical assistance contract will be combined with the
civil works contract for all 7 'riodic maintenance works for 1992 and 1993
and with a 142-km package of /iad rehabilitation works, giving the total
package an estimated val-- -f approximately US$18 million.
