C. Disbursements
4.14     Disbursements will be made against the following categories of
eligible expenditures: (a) 702 of total expenditures for civil works for
road rehabilitation and paving and engineering services for supervision of
such works; (b) 70? of total expenditures for periodic road maintenance
until disbursements reach US$7 million: 60? until disbursements reach US$10
million; and 50o thereafter; (c) 70% of total expenditures for
reconstruction of workshops; (d) OOZ of foreign expenditures and 902 of
local expenditures for purchase of equipment and spare parts and materials
for markings and signaling; (e) 75? of total expenditures for equipment
rehabilitation by contract; and (f) 10OZ of the cost for technical
assistance. The declining share of financing under (b) above is to achieve
an increasing participation by SEOPC in financing periodic maintenance
works.
4.15     The estimated schedule of disbursements is shown in Table 14.  The
Government's aim is to substantially complete the project by the end of
1994, and the implementation schedule has been developed on this basis.
Experience shows, however, that some slippage is likely. The Third
Reconstruction Project, Loan 2609-DO. will take five years to disburse
compared to the four years originally estimated. The estimated
disbursement profile adopted extends disbursements through mid-1995. The
project closing date is December 31, 1995.
4.16     Past experience with Special Accounts in the Dominican Republic
has not been favorable. The accounts have been operating only in local
currency and SEOPC has been unable tc use the account to pay for foreign
exchange obligatioas. Agreement was reached during negotiations on a
modality which, will allow the Special Account to operate in foreign currecy
and a Special Account in the amount of US$8 million will be established
under the Loan. Items costing up to USS25,000 for equipment, spare parts
and road signs, and US$100,000 for civil works will be submitted to the
Bank using Statements of Expenditures.
D. Audits
4.17     All records relating to project expenditures will be kept by the
project administration unit within DARE (para. 4.01). In previous Bank
loans, project expenditures have been audited by ii-dependent private
auditors, and these audit reports have generally been submitted on
schedule. The audits have contained no qualifications. During
negotiations, agreement was reached that SEOPC will have its records of
project expenditures. Statements of Expenditures and Special Account
audited in accordance with appropriate principles applied by independent
auditors acceptable to the Bank. The audit will be submitted no later than
five months after the end of each fiscal year.
E. Monitoring
4.18     The requirement for annual updating of the Five-Year Highway
Investment Program (para. 3.04) and the requirement to report on the road
maintenance budget and actual expenditures (para. 3.08), will provide an
opportunity to monitor the success in achieving the project's objectives.



- a-1 -
In addition, the consultants supervising the execution of civil works will
produce quarterly reports on the progress of each contract. Finally, SEOPC
will submit to the Bank a quarterly progress report describing the progress
of each of the loan components (para. 4.05).
F. Environmental Impact
4.19     SEOPC was authorized by Law No. 123, dated Hay 10, 1971 to
establish a Department for the Control of Earth Excavation (DCCT). DCCT
has regional representatives in ;.intiago, San Pedro de Macoris and Azua
responsible for the monitoring of environmental pollution in the northern,
eastern and the southern parts of the country, respectively. DCCT is
entrusted with protecting the ecology and environment through prevention of
indiscriminate excavation. The responsibility of DCCT is to regulate
excavation permits to prevent indiscriminate excavation and destruction of
agricultural areas, beaches, and rivers; to document environmental
implications of every request for excavation; and to monitor earth
excavation works after permits are issued and stop excavation works which
are improperly executed and impose fines on violators. DCCT is well
organi d and capable of carrying out the tasks under its responsibility.
The present limiting fuctor on DCCT activities is the lack of logistic
support. The proposed project includes funds to purchase needed equipment.
4.20     The DCCT is staffed with well qualified and dedicated
professionals capable of carrying out their programmed assignments.
Permanent higher staff is composed of three environmental specialists who
have been trained in major European and Asian countries. Specialists
required in other technical disciplines needed to complement DCCT's
environmental work are recruited from other government departments on an
as-needed basis.
4.21     The civil works under the project involve the rehabilitation of
the existing infrastructure and will not affect adversely the quality of
environmental or ecological systems. The works are designed to improve the
roads' drainage, their rolling surfaces, and to protect the roads' slopes
against erosion. The resulting reduction of heavy stop-and-go traffic will
reduce emissions associated with long idling times and repeated
acceleration; improve fuel economy; reduce rolling noise and vibrations;
and control sedimentation that might result from erosion and create a
potential for water pollution. Documents for all civil works contracts
customarily include a provision which obliges the co1itractors to dispose
properly of all debris and pollutants.
G.  Project Risks
4.22     This is the fifth Bank-financed road rehabilitation project in the
Dominican Republican and it has been prepared based on the experience
gained in the previous projects. The main obstacle to bringing the highway
network to acceptable standards has been a weak maintenance organization.
A maintenance management system installed under the previous Bank loans has
not been properly implemented. If this is allowed to continue, the major
effort put into rehabilitation in recent years will be partially wasted.
It is to minimize this risk that proposed project focuses on implementation
of a thorough maintenance program. The initial five-year plan has been



- 28 -
drawn up and agreed on. Further agreements were reached during
negotiations on the resources to be allocated to maintenance, improvements
in budget procedures to ensure that allocated resources end up in
maintenance activities, and strengthening of the maintenance organization
both at the central level and in the field. Finally, consultants will be
recruited to overview and assist in the implementation of the road
maintenance plan.
4.23     The second risk emanates from institutional constraints that can
retard project implementation. The lack of sufficient engineers to prepare
the final engineering designs could cause possible delays in preparation of
bid documents and project completion. To reduce this risk, the project
includes a provision for private engineering firms to carry out a major
part of the engineering designs. Also. the bidding documents for a major
contract involving all periodic maintenance, technical assistance in paving
and some rehabilitation works would be ready by negotiations, thus enabling
a quick start for the project. Finally, the stricter criteria for pre-
qualification of contractors will ensure that the selected firms are
capable of completing the works on schedule.
4.24     The high turnover of staff within the SEOPC requires repetition of
training programs and threatens the institutional improvement program.
However, this issue cannot be fully resolved within the proposed project,
since it is part of the Government's overall policy in regard to public
sector compensation. As long as salaries remain low, departure of staff to
the private sector will continue, with negative impact on project
execution. The extensive training programs, including overseas, funded
under the project provide some incentives to SEOPC staff to remain on the
job.
4.25     Availability of government funds could potentially become an issue
if the Government has to embark on a major budget expenditure reduction
program. However, the level of government support required under the
agreed five-year investment plan is in line with total government support
to the road sector in the past. With the completion of several large
projects undertaken by the Government in recent years, total local funding
for the sector could be reduced while still carrying out the agreed
investment plan. It should be noted, however, that Government funding for
the ongoing Bank project has not been an issue and that the Government will
commit itself under the loan to provide the specific amount of local
contribution to the project on a year-by-year basis.

td) The Borrower will take all actions to ensure that the costs
of road maintenance are borne by road users (para. 2.25);
(e) The revised prequalification document, agreed during
negotiations, will be used to prequalify all contractors
under the project (pars. 2.34);
(f) SEOPC will carry out the 1992-96 Highway Investment Program
according to the list of roads agreed on with the Bank and
included in the document "Programa de Rehabilitacidn de
Carreteras 1902-96". Any modification or addition of other
roads will be done only after obtaining the Bank's approval
(para. 3.04);
tg) The 1992-96 Highway Investment Program, as agreed with the
Bank, will be updated annually and presented to the Bank by
September 30 of each year (para. 3.04);
(h)  SEOPC will implement the 1992-96 Highway Maintenance Program
as detailed in the document "Programa de Mantenimiento de
Carreteras 1992-96" and the Government will allocate the
funds necessary to implement this program (para. 3.08);
(i) SEOPC will present to the Bank, by September 30 of each year,
a satisfactory updating of the road maintenance budget for
the next fiscal year, and by March 31 of each year will
report to the Bank the actual budget, by category of
expenditures, spent for maintenance in the preceding fiscal
year (para. 3.08);
(j) The Government will earmark the counterpart funds needed for
the project for 1992-94, (para. 3.27) as follows:
1992 - US$10.5 million equivalent.
1993 - US$10.7 million equivalent.
1994 - US$10.8 million equivalent.
(k) SEOPC will request the Bank's approval before contracting any
investment under the project if final engineering estimates
exceed the original cost estimate by more than 25Z in US
dollar terms (para. 3.31).
5.02     An action program will be agreed on with SEOPC to improve its
institutional structure, expedite project execution, and expand planning
capacity.   It will include:
(a) adding an engineer and an assistant engineer to DGM's
planning unit by December 31, 1991 (para. 2.09);
(b) nominating an engineer in charge of highway maintenance in
each of the DGM's 21 sub-districts by December 31, 1991
(para. 2.09);



- 30 -
(c) creating a separate planning and control unit for rural roads
within DGM by December 31, 1991 (para. 2.10);
(d) transferring the responsibility of traffic counts to the
General Directorate for Planning and Programming by December
31, 1991 and providing sufficient staff to carry out this
task (para. 2.15);
(e) purchasing portable scale for vehicle weight control and
placing them in operation by December 31, 1991 (para. 2.18);
(f) creating a superintendency for workshops by December 31, 1991
and staffing it adequately (para. 3.10)
(g) creating an equipment management unit within DGM by December
31, 1991, and staffing it adequately (para. 3.11):
(h) completing engineering designs for roads to be started in
1993 by December 31, 1991 and for roads to be started in
1994, by December 31, 1992 (para. 4.02); and
(i) submitting quarterly reports to the Bank (on March 31, June

implementation of all civil works contracts and other project
components including latest cost estimates; (c) the status of

and the amount actually spent; and (e) the actual road
maintenance budget for the preceding year, the budget
allocated for the current year and physical progress of the
maintenance plan for the year (para. 4.05).
Recommendation
5.03     The proposed project constitutes a suitable basis for a Bank loan
of US$79 million equivalent on the terms and conditions set out in chapters
I-IV of this report.

ANNEX 1
Page 1 of 4
DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
FIFTH ROAD REHABILITATION AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
Highway Maintenance
Highway Network
1.       The highways network under the responsibility of SEOPC totals
about 4,935 km of which 3,037 km (61.5? of the network) are paved, 1,162 km
(23.6? of the network) are not paved and 736 Km (14.9? of the network) are
under construction, reconstruction or rehabilitation. This network is
divided functionally into three categories: Trunk roads (primary network),
regional (secondary network) and local (tertiary network). They -are,
respectively, 21.9?, 46.22 and 31.9Z of the entire network. Mairntenance of
-:his network is carried out by the General Directorate of Maintenance
iDGM). Description of DGM can be found in Annex 4.
Planning and Budgeting
2.       Maintenance planning is not practiced.  Traditionally, the data
for budget preparation has been generate4 in DGM, with very little
quantitative input from the field operating units. The budget format is
very general and is not based on any measure of expected work performance.
Each year the budget submission represents a percentage increase over the
previous year, but with little assessment of what funds are really required
for each maintenance function.
3.       In addition to the unsatisfactory budget process, the release and
allocation of funds is a continuing problem. The figures officially
approved in the budget and the final allocation of funds to maintenance
differ substantially. The funds for maintenance remain at the Secretary's
disposition and can be allocated to carry out construction works and works
on urban or rural roads that do not belong to the SEOPC network. The lack
of the essential link between planned work and actual funding makes fiscal
management very difficult. This, coupled with delays in releasing of funds
from the National Budget Office, prevents the districts froip planning their
work properly. SEOPC fiscal financial management consists of assigning
funds to meet the most urgent demands without following established budget
procedures and without keeping adequate records. This produces an
additional difficulty in evaluating how much is really allocated to road
maintenance.
4.       The amounts spent by the end of the year are frequently above the
approved maintenance budget. Table 1 shows the programmed and actually
spent budgets for maintenance from 1983 to 1987. The table shows the large
differences between those figures and the dramatic decrease of the
maintenance budget between 1986 and 1987. This decrease is due to the
dismissal of about 6000 out of 12000 people from the maintenance forces and
the corresponding decrease in the budget allocation for materials and



- 51 -                      ANNEX 1
Page 2 of 4
equipment. These facts caused maintenance levels to deteriorate
significantly and so far have not recovered in real terms. The figures
corresponding to the budgets proposed by DGM, approved by the Government
and actually spent by DGM in 1988 and 1989 are the followingt
Year       Proposed ($RD)       Approved (SRD)      Spent ($RD)
1988       83,381,152           25,'91,000          37,820,000
1989       64,444,339           30,000,000          48,510,805
5.       In 1989, given an exchange rate of 6.35$RD per US dollar, the
average expenditure on highway maintenance was over US$1500 per km. This
is adequate for routine maintenance alone, but not enough to cover periodic
maintenance. In addition, we must take into account that the funds spent
for maintenance serve to pay about 6000 people who appear as DGM employees,
although only around 3500 people are actually working on maintenance works.
Finally, 1988 and 1989 allocations were only about 8? of the total spent
for roads, capital and current budgets, including works on urban roads and
streets. This rate is very low and confirms the need to prepare adequate
maintenance programs and budgets and to establish an appropriate control
system.
6.       To improve maintenance planning and budgeting, one needs to
update and put into practice the maintenance management system, which was
designed with funds provided under previous Bank loans. The system will
require updating data concerning effective work force for maintenance
activities, list of equipment and condition, and highway inventory by
physical characteristics. The implementation of the maintenance management
system will provide the means to improve the budget process. The
maintenance work program will establish requirements for labor, equipment
and materials. Consequently, for the first time SEOPC will be able to
submit a highway maintenance budget based on specific types and quantities
of work to be accomplished.
Highways Maintenance Programs (1991-1996).
7.       During the appraisal process, the maintenance activities and the
parameters of the existing management system were reviewed and updated for
planning purposes and used as inputs for the system in preparing
maintenance programs for the period 1991-1996. The first maintenance
program studied was the program for 1990, for which twenty nine maintenance
activities were selected. For each activity a work measurement unit (e.g.
square meter) was assigned. The level of maintenance service to be
provided was established for what was considered a full level of
maintenance service. This 'full level" implies all maintenance
requirements except marks and signals (studied separately, see Annex 5) and
was defined by a set of quantity standards (e.g. square meters of patching
per year) with a separate standard for each activity. Quantity standards
were fixed starting from the existing system and the results of inter.iews
with SEOPC's staff at the field level. Three basic levels of quantity
standards were determined corresponding to three categories of roads.
Modifying factors to the quantity standards were introduced according to
pavement condition, surface type, climatic regions, pavement age and DGM's
policies (e.g. manual or mechanical execution). Tables 2.A and 2.B show
the quantity standards and modifying factors used to prepare the 1992



- 52 -
AMX 1
Page 3 of 4
program. Once maintenance activities were selected and the levels of
maintenance were expressed in quantity standards, annual work quantities
were determined for each maintenance activity by multiplying the quantity
standard (which quantifies the level of service), by the appropriate
inventory quantity. The amounts and types of resources required were
determined by using each maintenance activity's performance standard.
These performance standards define the activity, the daily resource
requirements, the expected daily accomplishment and the approved work
method. They will also serve to monitor the execution of the programs and
to adjust future unit costs and daily accomplishment. Annual costs for
each activity were determined by multiplying the resource quantities by
unit costs for labor, equipment and materials as determined by the
Maintenance Planning Unit (NPU).
8.       The sum of all annual activity cost is the maintenance budget.
The network length considered under maintenance in the 1990 program was
3,884 km. corresponding to the length of the total highways network less
about 736 km under reconstruction or rehabilitation in 1990. The
maintenarze work program and budget were prepared for each highway
maintena!nce *ayudantia' (sub-district divisions) and district. As a sum of
these programs, . general program and budget were prepared for the nation's
entire highway network, which is shown in Tables 3.A and 3.B (Table 3.A
shows the annual budget and Table 3.B the resources needed and available
for carryin! out the works planned by SEOPC's force account). The total
maintenance budget for 1990 is of RD$ 75.3 million, equivalent to US$9.9
million of which RD$50.S million (US$6.6 million) are for activities
considered as routine maintenance Lnd RD$24.8 million (US$3.3 million) for
activities considered as periodic maintenance. The program for 1990 is a
step forward in improving road maintenance procedures and modifying current
maintenance practices and set up a starting point for developing the
maintenance programs of the following years.
9        The program for 1991 was prepared on the same basis that the 1990
program but, including under routine maintenance the 736 km. under
reconstruction and rehabilitation in 1990. The total maintenance budget
for 1991 is RD$81.9 million, equivalent to US$10.8 million of which RD$55.6
million (US$7.3 million) are for activities of routine maintenance and
RD$26.3 million (US$3.5 million) for activities of periodic maintenance.
The program for 1991 (shown in Table 4.A, annual budget. and Table 4.B,
resources needed and available for carrying out the works planned by
SEOPC's force account) is considered satisfactory and has been proposed to
SEOPC as a basis to prepare the 1991 maintenance budget.
10.      The programs for 1992, 1993, 1994  1995 and 1996 were prepared on
the same basis as that of the 1990 and 1991 programs.  They are also based
on the assumption that a total of 1,286 km will be reconstructed or
rehabilitated during the project period at a rate of 298 km in 1992, 300 km
in 1993, 235 km in 1994, 220 km in 1995 and 233 km in 1996. It was.
estimated that highways under reconstruction or rehabilitation in the year
considered will not need neither routine nor periodic maintenance in that
year. but will need routine maintenance in the following year and periodic
maintenance after four years of the reconstruction or rehabilitation works.
Another assumption was that appropriate equipment for mechanical vegetation
control will be acquired by SEOPC in 1992 under the proposed loan, to avoid
the large expenditures on manual vegetation control. The cost for this



- 53 -                       ANNEX I
Page 4 of 4
activity in the 1991 and 1992 programs reaches about RD$7.5 million
(US$950,000) annually, due to the high frequency of this activity, as a
consequence of the large rainfall and climatic conditions. In the 1993
maintenance program, the total cost for this activity is RD$2.5 million
(US$330,000), assuming that 65Z of vegetation control is done mechanically
(with the units acquired in 1991) and 352 is done manually. The cost of
the equipment to be acquired in 1992 is about US$600.000 (20 units at
US$30,000 each) showing that this equipment is highly profitable.
Therefore, acquisition of these units is included in this project.
11.      The results of the 1992-1996 maintenance programs are shown in
tables 5.A and B (1992); 6.A and B (1993); 7.A and B (1994); 8.A and B
(1995), and 9.A and B (1996). Tables type A show the partial budgets for
routine and periodic maintenance and the total maintenance budget for each
year. Tables type B show the resources needed and available for carrying
out the works planned by SEOPC's force account. Table 10 shows a summary
of the results of tables type A. As this project is expected to be
implemented in two phases, 1992-1994 and 1995-1996, table 10 shows the
totals for the first phase (1992-1994) and the whole period studied
(1992-1996). During the first phase (1992-1994) the total amount for
maintenance is RD$287.2 million, equivalent to US$37.8 million, of which
RD$147.0 million (US$19.3 million) are for routine maintenance, or an
average of US$1,300 per km per year, and RD$140.3 (US$18.4) are for
periodic maintenance, equivalent to US$1,240 per km per year. The length of
the network to be sealed in this phase is 804.4 km. The corresponding
figures for the total period (1992-1996) ares RD$451.3 (US$59.4 million)
for total maintenance; RD$241.9 million (US$31.8 million) for routine
maintenance, or US$1,285 per km per year; and RD$209.4 nillion (US$27.5
million) for periodic maintenance, equivalent to US$1,110 per km. per
year). The length of the network to be sealed in the entire period is
1,272 km. All these figures are considered adequate.
12.      The maintenance programs also define annual requirements of labor,
equipment and materials. The data on labor requirements is analyzed in
Annex 4 and the equipment requirements in Annex 3. However, SEOPC's own
resources are not sufficient to carry out both routine and periodic
maintenance. Therefore, most periodic maintenance will have to be carried
out by contract. Since this project includes a component for technical
assistance to improve SEOPC's technology know-how in road surface
treatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays (see Annex 6), we consider
the two maintenance activities related to surface improvement (bituminous
resealing and asphalt surface preparation or improvement) the most adequate
to be contracted. Therefore, we assume that these two maintenance
activlties in the 1992 and 1993 periodic maintenance programs (at a total
cost of US$10 million) will be included in the contract for technology
transfer (see annex 6, Tables I and 2). The same two activities included
in the 1994 periodic maintenance program (at a cost of US$3.7 million) will
be carried out 702 by contract and 30g by force account (see annex 6, Table
4) under supervision of the firm which will transfer the technology in
1992-93. The same two activities in the 1995 and 1996 periodic maintenance
programs will be carried out by contract and force account in the same
proportion as the 1994 program. These works by contract in the period
1992-94, at a total cost of US$13.7 million (RD$104.0 million), will be
financed by the new project.

DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
FIFTH ROAD REHABILITATION AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
Rural Roads
1.       The rural roads network under the responsibility of the SEOPC
comprises a total of 14,146 km out of which about 300 km. (12.12) are paved
with asphalt surface treatment. Maintenance of this network is carried out
by the General Directorate of Maintenance (DGM) through a sub-directorate
at the central level and by the same personnel and equipment which
maintains the highway network at the district level. (See Annexes 3 and 4).
2.       In 1985, 12,962 km of rural roads (91.61 of the network) were
invrentoried, showing that 34Z of the network was impassable. Since then,
the situation probably has improved, owing to works financed by AID and
IDB, but there is no data on the actual condition of the network. In
addition, the personnel in the districts is not even aware of all the rural
roads in their district which belong to the SEOPC network. Moreover, a
study financed by CIDA, under the supervision of IDB, considered that only
9,138 km (out of the total 14,146 km) were viable rural roads, or roads
with at least minimum engineering standards and not duplicated by parallell
roads. This means that about 5.000 km of rural roads, not considered by
the study as viable roads, could be excluded from the network to be
maintained by SEOPC. At present, DGM estimates that about 10 of the rural
roads network are only trails and cannot be considered as roads to be
maintained by SEOPC. Therefore, it is necessary as a first step to
determine the optimal size of the rural road network for future maintenance
plans. In addition, it is also necessary to review the state of each rural
road, given the reconstruction and rehabilitation works carried out from
the date of the last inventory, and the change in condition of other roads
due to weather, traffic, etc.
3.       IDB financed from 1980 to 1984 the reconstruction of 700 km of
feeder roads and the engineering designs of additional 2,000 km. At
present, IDB is financing the rehabilitation of 983 km. with two loans
amounting to US$20 million and total project cost of US$28.3 million. The
criteria to select the feeder roads to be reconstructed or rehabilitated
weres  i) provide access for agricultural products to regional markets; ii)
provide access for rural population to major urban areas; iii) alleviate
traffic conditions by improving the poor state of the roads; and iv) avoid
parallel roads in the same area. This project will be completed in 1990.
A new project is being prepared to rehabilitate another 1000 km. with a
loan of US$19.2 million and total project cost of US$27.5 million.



- 73 -
Page 2 of 3
4.       As a consequence of these projects, about 700 km of rural roads
are now asphalted; these 700 km. will meet better geometric and surface
conditions than many regional and local highways. For this reason, it will
be necessary to re-classify the entire network (highways and rural roads)
in accordance with the actual characteristics and functions of each road.
It is interesting to note that one of the main reasons for asphalting these
feeder roads has been the lack of maintenance service by SEOPC. This
factor has caused the paving of roads regardless of the traffic requirement
of a minimum of 150 vehicles per day which is needed to consider a road
asphaltable.
5.       In addition to the IDB projects, AID financed the rehabilitation
of 900 km of feeder roads between 1982 and 1987 and now is financing
routine maintenance of 2,000 km. This financing serves for the acquisition
of some small road equipment and tools. The counterpart funds are
allocated to pay about 700 roadmen. These people are appointed for routine
maintenance works by local communities (Agricultural Committees) and they
use the equipment and tools acquired under AID financing, but their
salaries are paid by SEOPC. Each roadman, with the corresponding tools,
maintain between 3.5 and 5 km. per annum and, consequently, a total of
about 2,500 to 3.500 km can be maintained every year using this method.
The roadmen carry out maintenance activities such as surface routine
maintenance (mainly patching with granular materials), drainage maintenance
and vegetation control. The AID project will be completed in 1990, but
this type of routine maintenance will be continued by SEOPC in the future.
6.       Periodic maintenance of rural roads is carried out by DGM's force
account but frequently is based only on user's claims or local authorities'
requests. Since there is no documented control of these activities, it is
not possible to know neither the resources allocated to this periodic
maintenance nor the length of the network annually maintained.
7.       In recent years, SEOPC has created new small mobile units, called
Oflotillas', for periodic maintenance of rural roads, working independently
of the ayudantlas (sub-district divisions), under the direct orders of the
district chiefs. At present, there are 15 of these *flotillasa working
satisfactorily and it is expected to increase this number in the future.
Maintenance activities carried out by "flotillas" are patching and surface
improvement, re-gravelling, and maintained ditches and culverts. Table 1
shows the units of each "flotilla" and Table 2 shows the annual operational
costs of this equipment, assuming 250 working-days of 8 hours and 70Z of
equipment utilization. It is estimated that each "flotilla' may carry out
20 km. a month of periodic maintenance, which means 3.600 km by year. As
in the case of routine maintenance, there is no program for this work.
Therefore, the length of the network maintained by 'flotillas" and roadmen
(para. 5 above) is between 6100 and 7100 km.
8.       The approved budget for current expenditures on rural roads for
1989 was RD$12 million (US$1.9 million or US$134 per km) for the whole road
rural network. However, as in the case of highways, there is no record of



- 74 -                     ANNEX 2
Page 3 of 3
the funds actually spent in maintenance activities. It is also very
difficult to know which rural roads received maintenance service with these
funds, because the sub-directorate for rural roads is practically non-
existent and its function is limited to organize and monitor the activities
of the roadmen involved in routine maintenance. However, it is estimated
that about 6.000 km of rural roads are receiving some maintenance on a
limited basis. The rest of the rural road network is practically
neglected.
9.       As a conclusion, maintenance programs prepared in a similar way to
those of the highways network are needed to carry out adequate rural road
maintenance programs. To do this, a first step is to update the rural road
inventory, establishing the physical condition of the whole network. A
second step is to re-classify the roads, transferring to the highways
network the rural roads that have sufficiently high standards to be
considered regional or local highways and excluding from the rural road
network those rural roads which are only trails or duplicate unnecessarily
other roads. For these two tasks, the project includes financing for
technical assistance, estimated at US$200,000.

DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
FIFTH ROAD REHABILITATION AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
Road Maintenance Eauipment and Workshops
Equipment Fleet
1.       The current SEOPC road maintenance fleet is composed of 556 units
of which 165 (30?) are less than 3 years old; 138 (251) between 4 and 6
years old; 161 (292) 7 to 10 years old; and 92 (16Z) 11 to 15 years old.
At present, 239 units (43?) are out of service and need rehabilitation or
major repairs, Table 1 shows the various equipment groups and the current
condition of each group. In addition, units to be added to SEOPC's fleet
during 1990 are being acquired. Table 2 contains the list of this new
equipment. This fleet can be adequate to carry out the highways
maintenance programs for 1991-1996, if it is maintained and rehabilitated
in a proper and timely manner. With the exception of the units for
mechanical vegetation control described in Annex 1, new equipment will not
be needed during the life of this project, unless new units are justified
by future adjustments of the maintenance programs. The cost of the 20 new
units for mechanical control vegetation will be about US$600,000, financed
under this project. The oldest units of equipment which can not be
rehabilitated economically, will be sold at auction or discarded.
Equipment Management
2.       At present, equipment management is poor.  Many relatively new
units (4-5 years old) are in poor condition because they are not properly
operated and maintained. SEOPC's capital investment in equipment must be
better protected by observing regular preventive maintenance. The first
steps in this direction have been taken with technical assistance financed
with Loan 2609-DO. In addition, the existing structure for equipment
administration must be strengthened. Annex 4 contains a proposal for
improving DGM's current personnel organization, including equipment
administration.
3.       In the future, each equipment unit should preferably be assigned
to a specific operator who will be responsible for its operation and
maintenance. These operators should be trained under a program carried out
within the SEOPC. Due to the lack of tenure in operators' positions, the
program should be permanent. The training will serve to improve the
present quality of equipment operation and maintenance. This project
includes a component of RD$2.3 million equivalent to US$300,000, to finance
the proposed training program.
4.       Furthermore, an equipment management system is needed to carry out
the equipment rehabilitation, repair and maintenance programs. Financing
for the implementation of a maintenance and equipment management system
throughout the country is included in the project at a cost of US$800,000
over a period of two years. Annex 7 contains the proposed terms of
reference for this technical assistance.



- 78 -                     ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 3
Equipment Rehabilitation
5.       Equipment rehabilitation can save SEOPC substantial resources.  In
view of its recent equipment purchases, SEOPC should not buy new units
until the rehabilitation of its equipment is completed. An equipment
rehabilitation and major repairs program has been prepared by DGM with the
assistance of a specialist financed under Loan 2609-DO. This program
details the equipment rehabilitation and major repairs during the period
1992-94 and was prepared to include all units that will be more than two
years old in 1991. It is based on the needs arising from the 1992-94 road
maintenance programs.   Table 3 shows the list of units to be rehabilitated
in the project period. The cost of the program is estimated at US$4
million. It is expected that units in service more than 2 years in
1994-1995 will need rehabilitation in 1995-96, at an additional cost of
about US$2 million. As a result, the whole program of rehabilitation
during the period 1992-1996 could reach a total of US$6 million. The
project includes a component of RD$30.4 million, equivalent to US$4
million, to finance the equipment rehabilitation program for 1992-94.
6.       Ideally, some equipment rehabilitation should be carried out in
SEOPC workshops. This would be possible if SEOPC workshops had proper
tools, workshop equipment and an adequate organization. The first two
conditions have basically been met (Loan 2609-DO provided funds for it) but
SEOPC's workshop organization is still the limiting factor in carrying out
a program of such magnitude. Until this situation improves, rehabilitation
will have to be carried out by contract in local suppliers' workshops,
leaving only preventive maintenance and minor repairs to be done by SEOPC.
This contracting system will have an additional advantage of preventing an
increase in SEOPC staff. Another possible arrangement would be allowing
SEOPC mechanics to work in the contractor workshops, which could reduce the
cost to SEOPC and provide mechanics with valuable on-the-job training.
7.       The rehabilitation and major repair contracting system would be
further expanded and strengthened under this project. Table 4 shows the
performance of several local suppliers' workshops in which DGM equipment
units have been rehabilitated or repaired, largely with financing under
Loan 2609-DO. The performance has been acceptable or good. It is
interesting to note the good results achieved by the supplier Astilleros
Navales Dominicanos, a public firm, which started rehabilitatir.g SEOPC's
equipment in 1989, under Loan 2609-DO. Its unit prices are lower than
those of private firms. The firm buys spare parts after comparing price
quotations obtained from three local suppliers and awards contracts to the
lowest bidder. Its equipment and personnel, as well as the quality of the
work executed by the firm, are satisfactory. It is estimated that about 40
units of equipment could be rehabilitated annually in the Astilleros
Navales Dominicanos' workshop, an important contribution to the
rehabilitation program. Other firms to be considered for equipment
rehabilitation could be local equipment distributors, if their
capabilities, facilities and experience are acceptable.
Equipment Operational Costs and Repair
8.       During the project period, large resources will be needed to
operate the road maintenance equipment. Table 5 summarizes the
requirements for fuel, lubricants, tires and spare parts to operate SEOPC's



- 79 -                  ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 3
equipment in the period 1992-1996. To ensure that sufficient funds are
available, financing for acquisition of tires and spare parts for repairs
has been included in this project at a cost of RD$98.9 million, equivalent
to US$7.6 million, for the period 1992-94 (US$12.9 million are needed for
the total period 1992-96). Close supervision will be required to monitor
allocated resources and to ensure that they are applied effectively.
9.       Minor repairs included in the equipment repair programs will be
carried out by SEOPC's and local suppliers workshops that have adequate
facilities. In many cases, these minor repairs could be done efficiently,
at low costs, by contracting mechanics operating small workshops. In these
cases, SEOPC would provide the necessary spare parts and supplies and the
contractor would provide the mechanics. Since the work capacity of the
contracted mechanics and the workshops is limited, flexible procurement
procedures would be used to award contracts for the repair of SEOPC
equipment. DGM would be responsible for the work done using this
arrangement.
Workshop Organization and Needs
10.      SEOPC's work3hops are classified according to their geographical
location, and type and complexity of mechanical work to be performed. At
present, there are two workshops designated as class "Al, seven as class
"B' and nine as class "C". All types of repairs are carried out in the
class "A". Workshops of class "B' are used to perform repairs of minor
components. Class "Al and "BO also carry out regular preventive
maintenance for their assigned equipment units. Class 'C" workshops only
carry out preventive maintenance tasks such as oil changes, greasing,
adjustments, etc.
11.      Adequate workshop facilities and competent personel are essential
to maintaining the equipment fleet in good condition. Loan 2609-DO
financed tools and operating facilities for all SEOPC's workshops.
However, some workshops still need to improve its buildings and other
facilities at a cost estimated at RD$19.5 million, equivalent to US$ 1.5
million (US$ 1,350,000 for the workshops and US$ 150,000 for the central
offices). Table 6 shows a breakdown of the resources that will be needed
to construct adeaquate buildings for three "C" workshops and one "B"
workshop and to rehabilitate the central workshop and three 'A' workshops.

DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
FIFTH ROAD REHABILITATION AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
Road Maintenance Staffing
1.      The DGM employs about 6,000 people of which 1200 work at the
central office in Santo Domingo and the rest work in four maintenance
districts. Geographic distribution of these districts is adequate. The
districts are further sub-divided into 'ayudantlas*. The central district
of Santo Domingo and the district of Santiago which are the most important,
have 6 'ayudantiasa each; the district of Azua has 5 layudantlas" and the
district of La Vega has 4 sayudantiasg. Each wayudantial employs about 200
people.
2.      The number of employees at the DGM appears excessive; yet a closer
examination reveals that about 201 of staff is working in areas not related
to maintenance activities and the rate of absenteeism reaches more than 5Z.
The analysis considers only the personnel actually working in maintenance
at the central office and at the districts.
3.      The present organization of DGM is shown in Table 1.  Basic changes
in this organizational structure are not necessary to. improve highway
maintenance. However, some minor changes and additional staff are needed.
We proposed the following: (see Table 2)
Mi)   create, under the Road Maintenance Department (reporting
directly to the General Director of DGM) a Rural Roads
Maintenance Planning Unit (separately from the Highways
Maintenance Planning Unit).  The new unit will be charged
with planning, execution and control of the rural road
maintenance program. This unit should be staffed with a
chief-engineer, two assistant engineers and support staff;
(ii) create, within the Technical Sub-Directorate for Equipment,
an Equipment Management Unit (EMU), charged with the
implementation of the Equipment Management System (to be
implemented with technical assistance included in this
project, see Annex 7) and a Technical and Administrative
Support U:ait (TASU), charged with technical and
administrative support to EMU. This should be staffed with a
chief-engineer, three assistant engineers and support staff;
TASU should be staffed with a chief-engineer, one assistant
engineer, one administrative officer and support staff.
These new units will replace the existing units of Equipment
Reevaluation and Equipment Preventive Maintenance and the
planned but not yet created Equipment Supervision Unit.
Staff of the existing units will be transferred to the new
ones.



- 87 -                               ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 2
(iii) staff the Workshop Superintendence, in charge of coordination
and supervision of DGM's workshops, with a workshop
superintendent, three assistant engineers and support staff;
and,
(iv)  strengthen the Highways Maintenance Planning Unit, providing
two additional assistant engineers and support staff.
4.      Concerning district personnel, Table 3 shows DGM's personnel
actually working in maintenance, by district and category. There are 3500
DGM people working in the districts (at the field, workshops and offices)
enough to carry out the maintenance programs presented in Annex 1.
However, there is a pressing need to strengthen this work force with
technical and support staff. In this respect, the organization should be
strengthened by assigning to each district an engineer and support staff,
who will be responsible for planning, execution and monitoring at district-
level of the proposed maintenance programs and for the coordination with
the *ayudantlas" and with the central office.
5.      A serious problem concerning the SEOPC work-force, in general, and
technical personnel, in particular, is a lack of tenure in their positions.
As a result, SEOPC engineers, specialists, foremen, and other field and
office personnel are substituted by newly appointed people with each change
in Government. This represents a loss of qualified personnel, often
trained at great expense to the Government, and creates serious problems
for the implementation of new techniques in the management and execution of
the required maintenance activities, including planning and field work.
These problems are especially acute at the level of the district chiefs and
ayudantiasu, which are directly responsible for road maintenance. The
problem is further compounded by low salaries paid to SEOPC employees. The
salaries are inadequate and cause a high turnover among capable
technicians. If this departure of staff continues, it may ;mpact
negatively on project execution. Extensive training programs, included
under this project, will provide some incentive to SEOPC's staff to remain
on the job.   The amount included in the project for these programs is
US$200,000 equivalent.

- 91 -                            Page 1 of 1
DOMINICAN REPUBLIC
FIFTH ROAD REHABILITATION AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
Road Marking and Signaling
1.      The number of accidents on the highway network is high, resulting
in heavy loss of life and property. One of the causes of the accidents is
the lack of adequate vertical signalization and pavement marking. At
present, only 55X of the highway network has pavement marking. Out of the
remaining 45%, less than a half has pavement marking in good state. The
vertical signalization is even in a worse condition: even heavy-traffic
roads of the major trunk network, such as the Duarte Highway, have no
preventive signals.
2.      Roads under reconstruction will be marked and signposted before
works are completed. However, the standards and materials used to date
have not been satisfactory.  The length of roads marked and signposted
during the period 1987-89 is shown in the following table:
Year                        Marking (Km)              Signaling (Km)
1987                            223.1                      76.5
1988                            203.7                     262.0
1989                             74.6                     221.1
Total                           501.4                     559.6
3.      To improve the present situation, a program for marking and
signaling for the period 1992-1996 has been prepared. Table 1 shows the
five types of projects proposed for marking with the average per kilometer
costs for each type of a project. lables 2, 3, 4, 5 and 6 show this
program in detail, whose total cost is equivalent to US$900,000 in the
period 1992-1996. Some 91.5Z out of this amount corresponds to material
cost, 5.12 to equipment and the remaining 3.3? to labor costs (see Table
7). The project includes a component equivalent to US$600,000, for
financing marking and signaling in the period 1992-94.

DOMINICAN REPUPLIC
FIFTH ROAD RERABILITATION AND MAINTENANCE PROJECT
Terms of Reference for the Technoloav Transfer Proaram
for Surface Treatments and Thin Asphalt Concrete Overlags
General
1. One of the constraints that h.as prevented adequate periodic maintenance
in the Dominican Republic has been the lack of expertise with advanced
technologies related to surface treatments of bituminous-paved roads. Such
treatments should be carried out as normal periodic maintenance activity to
prevent deterioration and to extend the economic life of these paved roads.
The lack of expertise affects not only the Secretariat of Public Work.'
(SROPC) maintenance effort but also the domestic construction industry.
2.   The proposed technical assistance program will be geared to technical
personnel and manual labor from SEOPC' Directorate for Highway Maintenance
(DGM) and from domestic construction firms that are registered by SEOPC as
qualified for road pavement works. The program is to be carried out mostly in
the field. Road sections in need of surface treatment or thin overlay will be
selected from SEOPC's rehabilitation and maintenance programs.
Program Obiectives
3.   Through a functional working and training scheme, the program will help
SEOPC, during three years, to upgrade the capability of the DGM and the
domestic construction industry to carry out bituminous surface treatments and
thin asphalt concrete overlays. The major program objectives ares
(i)   To ensure that the technical field staff of DGM and that of the
domestic construction industry acquire a sound knowledge of the
basic principles, design criteria and construction methods for
bituminous surface treatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays;
(ii)   To carry out surfece treatments and thins asphalt concrete overlays
under contract, improving current construction practices and
introducing new technologies; and
(iii)   To ensure that DGM's technical staff acquire the necessary
expertise to program, budget, im-plement, control and supervise
bituminous surface treatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays.



- 99 -
ANNEX 6
Page 2 of 10
ProRram Description
4.   To achieve its objectives the program will be carried out by one
specialized firm satisfactorily equipped to cover the entire process of
resurfacing, from the manufacture of the asphalt products to the execution of
the works. This firm should have specialists, researchers, laboratories,
etc., to teach and assist in implementing the technologies. The program will
be carried out in the period 1992-1994 and will be financed by the World Bank-
supported project.
5.   The specialized firm will directly execute the works on new technologies
and will engage local contractors for the conventional works, to be selected
from the rehabilitation program for 1992 and 1992. During these tw- years,
the firm will transfer technology on surface treatments and thin asphalt
concrete overlays to local firms and SEOPC's staff, who will execute, in turn,
the works of the 1994 Periodic Maintenance Program (702 by contract and 302 by
force account) under supervision of the International firm.
Proaram Components
6.   The technical aseistance program will have two component: (i) execution
of works in 1992 and 1993; and (ii) technical assistance to improve surface
treatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays and to supervise the works
carried out by selected local contractors antd SEOPC's staff (trained in 1992-
93 under the program) in 1994. These two components will be included in one
contract and awarded to one qualified specialized firm meeting the conditions
required in the bidding documents.
Scope of the Contract
7.   The proposed contract will include: (i) surface treatments of 552 km..
from the 1992 and 1993 periodic maintenance programs. Tables 1 (1992) and 2
(1993) show the lists of the works included; (ii) thin asphalt programs for
1992 and 1993; (iii) technical assistance in pavement technology on surface
treatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays in 1992 and 19931 and (iv)
superv4sion of periodic maintenance works carried out by local firms and
SEOPC's staff in 1994 (see Table 4).
8.   Out of the total amount for civil works in the contract, about US$2
million will be set aside for carrying out works using new technologies in
testing sections to be proposed by the specialized firms (among the road
sections in Table 3) during the phase of prequalification. The proposal will
be evaluated by SEOPC, in accordance with the evaluation clauses included in
the prequalification documents. The remaining amount will be allocated to
carry out conventional works in the road sections selected by SEOPC in the
bidding documents.



- 1N -
Page 3 of 10
Civil Works Cgoponent
9.   The conventional work units to be included in the contract will be the
following:
(a) Surface treatments without aggregates:
-    Prime coats
-    Tack coats
(b) Surface treatments with aggregates:
-    Simple treatments
-    Double treatments
-    Multiple treatments
-    Seals
(c) Slurry seals
(d) Cold asphalt concretes
(e) Hot asphalt concretes
In cases (a), (b), (c) and (d), asphalt emulsions will be used as binders; in
case (e) the binders will be asphalt bitumens. These works will be performed,
based on SEOPC's design and specifications, on a list of highways selected by
SEOPC from the rehabilitation and periodic maintenance programs. The contract
will be awarded through international competitive bidding (ICB) in accordance
with the Guidelines of the World Bank.
10. The work units to be includsd in the contractor's proposal for new
technologies will be the followingt
-    thin asphalt concrete layers using modified binders on .bout 20
kms., to be proposed by the firms (among the sections included in
Table 3) in the phase of prequalification.
-    porous or draining hot asphalt concrete with modified asphalt
bitumens as binders on about 20 kms. to be proposed by the
specialized firms (among the sections included in Table 3) in the
phase of prequalification.
11. The modified binders for these work units will be proposed by the
contractor and imported duty-free from the manufacturing countries. The works
will be carried out under the contractor's design and specifications. The
proposals will be evaluated taking into account quality, feasibility and price
(see paras. 21 and 22).



- 3.01 -
MM 6
Page 4 of 10
Technical Assistance Comgonent
12. The technical assistance will aim to design, manufacture, execute,
control and supervise surface treatments and thin asphalt concrete layers
(less than 500 mm. or 2 inches) carried out with asphalt emulsions and asphalt
bitumens, modified or not, as binders. The assistance vill include
specifications and defining the scope of these works.
13. The assistance in design will aim to improve technical procedures
comonly used by SEOPC engineers for determining the optimal proportion of
surface treatment and asphalt concrete. In particular, the assistance will
include: (i) selecting the appropriate treatment or asphalt concrete in each
case; (ii) selecting aggregates for the treatment of asphalt concrete; (iii)
selecting appropriate binders for the treatment of asphalt concrete; (iv)
determining the optimal emulsion or bitumen dosage; and (v) determining, if
applicable, the optimal mechanical characteristics of asphalt concretes.
14. The assistance in manufacturing will provide information about: (i)
appropriate equipment to manufacture the mixes and asphalt concretes; (ii)
criteria to select the appropriate equipment in each case; (iii) guidelines
for using this equipment; (iv) constraints on using treatments and asphalt
concretes; and (v) guidelines for appropriate compacting.
15. The assistance in execution of works will provide: (i) information about
the existing equipment which will be used to execute the contract; (ii)
criteria for selecting the appropriate equipment in each case; (iii)
guidelines for using this equipment; (iv) constraints on using treatments and
asphalt concretes; and (v) guidelines for appropriate compacting systems.
16. The assistance in control of works will provide technical guidelines for
executing the contract.
17. The assistance in supervision of works carried out by local contractors
(70%) and SEOPC's force account (30%) during the third year of the contract
will provide: (i) reviewing prequalification documents, bidding documents and
procedures, and contract awards; (ii) monitoring work quantity and quality;
(iii) certificating contractor's invoices; and (iv) receiving the works.
18. 2he technical assistance will also include a training program at office,
laboratory and field levels, in the central department and in the districts.
The training program will be directed towards training personnel in work
methods for surface treatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays, both in
theoretical and practical issues. The trainees will be selected by SEOPC from
its own personnel and from skilled personnel of the private domestic
construction industry, qualified by SEOPC as road pavement contractors, that
submit their applications to SEOPC and are accepted. The trainees from these
private firms who pass the training courses will be given certificates.  In
future bidding documents for procurement of surface treatments and thin
asphalt concrete overlays, SEOPC will include clauses requiring that the



- 102 -
ARME 6
Page 5 of 10
contractor provide this type of certified personnel for executing the wort's.
During the third year of the program (1994)9 surface treatments and thin
asphalt concrete overlays will be awarded by SEOPC to local firms which will
be required to use these trained personnel and 4pjropriate equipment.
19. As for the staff of the SEOPC that will be trained under the program,
the contractor will submit during the prequalification stage a proposal for a
training program and equipment needed so as to:
(i) improve the execution of the conventional works that will be
carried out directly by the SEOPC;
(ii) enable SEOPC to supervise contract execution of pavement works
after the technic 1 assistance is concluded; and
(iii) enable SEOPC to manage future periodic maintenance programs.
In order to ensure the effective participation of selected staff of SEOPC in
the training program, the contractor will include, in the bid offer, a
reimbursable amount to be used for payment of travel and subsistence expenses,
and auxiliary means.
20. The contractor will provide an appropriate laboratory at the central
level and will coaplete the existing laboratories at the district level to
carry out the program for monitoring the works and for training. The
laboratory at the central level will be a section of the existing SEOPC
laboratory and SEOPC will provide roc2 for it in the same building. This
section of the central laboratory will work only for surface treatments, thin
asphalt concrete overlays and training included in the contract. When the
program is completed, this section will be incorporated into SEOPC's Central
Laboratory.
Procurement
21. The SEOPC will invite for prequalification all local firms which
manufacture road binders and contractors of road paving works that wish to
participate in the program. The prequalification process will be carried out
using the standard prequalification document prepared by the SEOPC and
approved by the Bank. Prom among the prequalified local firms, the
specialized firms will select one local manufacturer of binders and four (one
per district) paving firms for thin asphalt concrete layers and four (one per
district) for surface treatments and slurry seals, which will directly benefit
from the transfer of technology. Given the special scope and objectives of
the contract, it will be expressly recommended that the specialized firms form
association with the selected local firms (joint ventures) in line with the
objectives of the program.
22. The specialized firms interested in the contract will be prequalified by
SEOPC in accordance with the prequalification documents previously approved by



- 103 -
Page 6 of 10
the Bank. At this stage, the firms will submit their proposal on technical
assistance and new technologies in the form required by the prequalification
documents expressly prepared for this contract. The prequalification process
should be based on the following criteria:
(i)   previous experience and performance record with respect to similar
works in the two components of the contract.   The contractoro must
justify that in the last five years they have carried out the
following works:
-    300 kms. of pavement with thin layers using conventional
asphalt concrete;
-    300 kms. of asphalt surface treatments;
-    200 kms. of  conventional slurry seal treatments;
-    100 kms. of thin asphalt concrete layers, using modified
emulsions as binders; and
-    100 kms. of macro slurry seals using modified emulsions as
binders;
Also, the contractors must have experience, as an enterprise or group of
enterprises, in the following fields:
-    supervision and quality control of paving vorks;
-    technical assistance to public entities or private enterprises
in the field of road paving with conventional and new
technologies;
-    research in the characteristics and applications of bituminous
binders, both in its manufacturing and practical applications;
-    execution, at least once, of a mixed program of construction
and technical assistance in a developing country;
(ii) financial resources according to the evaluation criteria specified
in the prequalification document;
(iii) staff experience, including a principal and an alternate for each
of the specialties indicated in para. 23 of these TOks and a
general project coordinator, all with at least 15 years of
experience; all other staff should have at least 10 years of
experienco;
(iv) proposed work program that details all the activities defined in
the TORe for the two components of the program and defines those
aspects of the technical assistance to be proposed by the
contractor;
(v) detailed proposal of the works to be carried out with new
technologies, in accordance with para. 10 of these TORs;



- 104 -
Page 7 of 10
(vi) equipment list, detailing the specialized equipment that will be
used in the execution of the program of the civil works, the
equipment that must be temporarily or permanently imported if it is
not available in the country, and the equipment expressly dedicated
to the execution of the civil works with new technologies. The
detailed equipment list should only include specialized equipment
or equipment that is considered essential for the execution of the
contract in its two components;
(vii) viability of the proposal, taking into consideration the work
programs, the assigned resources and the specific conditions in the
Dominican Republic;
(viii) cost of the technical assistance component and of the civil works
using new technologies, based on a detailed unit price study.
These unit prices, for technical assistance and civil works with
new technologies, cannot be altered in later stages of the bidding
process after the final bids offers for the entire program
(including conventional civil works) are submitted. (For
prequalification purposes, only the technical merits of the study
will be evaluated, not the final cost.)
Staffing
23. The technical assistance will be provided by the following specialists.
(i) a highway engineer, with experience in planning, programming and
supervising asphalt paving operations, who will be the program
leader and coordinator (42 months);
(ii)  a highway engineer, with background in pavements and materials and
with experience in construction of asphalt surface treatments and
thin asphalt concrete layers (24 months);
(iii) a laboratory specialist, vith experience in organization and
coordination of road material laboratories (36 months);
(iv) a construction specialist with experience in coordination and
execution of surface treatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays
(24 months); and
(v) a construction supervisor (civil engineer), with experience in
organization, execution and control of asphalt paving operations
(36 months).
24. It is envisaged that the technical assistance program will be carried
out over n period of three years and will require a total of 162 man-months.
All staff must be fluent in Spanish.

25. DON will be the executing Jgency. The permanent technical staff of DON
and SEOPC's Directorate for Highways and the skilled staff of local firms
selected by SEOPC will be recipients of the assistance. In addition, SEOPC
may consider including university graduates and engineers employed by local
consulting firms with which SEOPC has contractual relations.
26. The execution of the program will be carried out in the following
manner:
(a) conventional civil works, by local firms associated with, or
proposed by, the contractor from among the prequalified by the
SEOPC, four (one p2r district) for thin asphalt concrete layers and
other four (one per district) for surface treatments and slurry
seals works.
(b) civil works with new technologies, directly by the specialized firm
(or group of firms) awarded the contract.
(c)  technical assistance, directly by the specialized firm (or group of
firm.) awarded the contract.
(d)  the manufacturing of convent-nal binders (asphalt bitumen, fluid
bitumens and emulsions), by the local firm proposed by the
contractor, from among those prequalified by the SEOPC.
(e) the manufacture of special binder6, by the specialixed firm (or
group of firms) awarded the contract.
8chedule
27. The contact will be carried out in seven phases. In the first nhase,
the coordinator of the program will review current surrace treatment practices
employed by SEOPC snd by local firms working as road pavement contractors for
SEOPC, including, but not necessarily limited to, t:he following:
(a) design procedures;
(b)  technical specifications;
(c)  methods of construction, supervision and traffic control;
(d)  suitability and mechanical conditions of the equipment;
(e)  availability and suitability of materials;
(f)  methods of asphalt storage and heating; and



- 106 -
Page 9 of 10
(g) expertise of operators and medium level technicians.
28. In the second phase, upon completion of the review done under the first
phase, the consultants will prepare a specific program for civil works and
technical assistance, and will submit the program to SEOPC with supporting
documentation, including working papers with their findings and
recommendations for program implementation. The program on civil works will
cover, but no necessarily be limited to, the following:
-    final engineering design of surface treatments and thin asphalt
concrete overlays included in the contract;
-    final technical specifications ior executing the works included in
the contract;
-    Definite work program which will not exceed the time frame
established in the contract; and
-    Definitive list and work program of surface treatments and overlays
to be executed under new technologies.
29. The program of technical assistance will cover, but not necessarily be
limited to, the following:
(a) short-term seminars for SEOPC and contractors' staff. The state of
the art of bituminous surface treatments and thin asphalt concrete
overlays should be covered in full depth;
*b) on-the-job training conducted by the consultants. For this
purpose, works included in the contact will be selected. Emphasis
should be given to quality control of crushed rock aggregates and
asphalt materials, preparation of surfaces for bituminous surface
treatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays, application of
gravel chips and asphalt binders, compaction methods and traffic
control; and
(c) preparation of guidelines and practical manuals for sealings,
surface treatments, slurries and asphalt concretes.
30. In the third phase, SEOPC will discuss and agree on the proposed
programs with the consultants. The three initial phases will be carried out
in three months.
31. In the fourth nhase, the consultants will prepare the followings (i)
the final designs and technical specifications for civil works; (ii) written
material for personnel training; and (iii) lists of required improvements to
the existing laboratory facilities. This phase will be carried out in three
months.

32. The fifth staae will consist of the execution of the approved programs
for civil works and technical assistance to be carried out in 1992 und 1993
(24 months). The execution of the civil works with new technologies and the
technical assistance program vill be carried out directly by the specialized
firm awarded the contract. The execution of the conventional civil work. vill
be carriee out by selected local firms in accordance with par&. 21 of these
TORs.
33.  The sixth ghase will consist of supervising the periodic mainteu*uee
works carried out by local contractors in 1994 (with skilled personnel trained
under technical assistance during 1992 and 1993).
34. In the seventh and last phase (overlapping with the sixth phase) the
consultants vill: (i) evaluate the results of the program; (ii) formulate
recommendations for follow-up action; (iii) assist DGM until the end of the
program for 1992-94 in order to ensure the continuation of the tasks when the
consultants leave; and (iv) prepare the 1995-1996 program for surface
triatments and thin asphalt concrete overlays. The evaluation of the program
for 1992-94 and the preparation of the pro-ram for 1995-96, will be included
in the final report. During the period that services are rendered, the
consultant will submit quarterly reports to the DGM DirectrT General.
